I remember crossing in the\r\n_Mauretania_ once, and saying to the Captain\xe2\x80\x94Richards\xe2\x80\x94did you know\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Now tell me what perils you really dread most for your ship,\r\nCaptain Richards?\xe2\x80\x99 expecting him to say icebergs, or derelicts, or fog,\r\nor something of that sort. Not a bit of it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve always remembered his\r\nanswer. \xe2\x80\x98_Sedgius aquatici_,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, which I take to be a kind of\r\nduck-weed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Pepper looked up sharply, and was about to put a question when\r\nWilloughby continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve an awful time of it\xe2\x80\x94those captains! Three thousand souls on\r\nboard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. She turned to Helen with an air of\r\nprofundity. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m convinced people are wrong when they say it\xe2\x80\x99s work\r\nthat wears one; it\xe2\x80\x99s responsibility. That\xe2\x80\x99s why one pays one\xe2\x80\x99s cook\r\nmore than one\xe2\x80\x99s housemaid, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccording to that, one ought to pay one\xe2\x80\x99s nurse double; but one\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but think what a joy to have to do with babies, instead of\r\nsaucepans!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Dalloway, looking with more interest at Helen, a\r\nprobable mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d much rather be a cook than a nurse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing would\r\ninduce me to take charge of children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMothers always exaggerate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley. \xe2\x80\x9cA well-bred child is no\r\nresponsibility. I\xe2\x80\x99ve travelled all over Europe with mine. You just wrap\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em up warm and put \xe2\x80\x99em in the rack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelen laughed at that. Mrs. Dalloway exclaimed, looking at Ridley:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow like a father! My husband\xe2\x80\x99s just the same. And then one talks of\r\nthe equality of the sexes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes one?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Pepper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, some do!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cMy husband had to pass an irate lady\r\nevery afternoon last session who said nothing else, I imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe sat outside the house; it was very awkward,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dalloway. \xe2\x80\x9cAt\r\nlast I plucked up courage and said to her, \xe2\x80\x98My good creature, you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nonly in the way where you are. You\xe2\x80\x99re hindering me, and you\xe2\x80\x99re doing no\r\ngood to yourself.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then she caught him by the coat, and would have scratched his eyes\r\nout\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Dalloway put in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s been exaggerated,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I pity them, I\r\nconfess. The discomfort of sitting on those steps must be awful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cServe them right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willoughby curtly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m entirely with you there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dalloway. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody can condemn\r\nthe utter folly and futility of such behaviour more than I do; and as\r\nfor the whole agitation, well! may I be in my grave before a woman has\r\nthe right to vote in England! That\xe2\x80\x99s all I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe solemnity of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s assertion made Clarissa grave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s unthinkable,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me you\xe2\x80\x99re a suffragist?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nturned to Ridley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care a fig one way or t\xe2\x80\x99other,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ambrose. \xe2\x80\x9cIf any creature\r\nis so deluded as to think that a vote does him or her any good, let him\r\nhave it. He\xe2\x80\x99ll soon learn better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not a politician, I see,\xe2\x80\x9d she smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid your husband won\xe2\x80\x99t approve of me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dalloway aside, to\r\nMrs. Ambrose. She suddenly recollected that he had been in Parliament.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you ever find it rather dull?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, not knowing exactly\r\nwhat to say.\r\n\r\nRichard spread his hands before him, as if inscriptions were to be read\r\nin the palms of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you ask me whether I ever find it rather dull,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nbound to say yes; on the other hand, if you ask me what career do you\r\nconsider on the whole, taking the good with the bad, the most enjoyable\r\nand enviable, not to speak of its more serious side, of all careers,\r\nfor a man, I am bound to say, \xe2\x80\x98The Politician\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Bar or politics, I agree,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willoughby. \xe2\x80\x9cYou get more run for\r\nyour money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one\xe2\x80\x99s faculties have their play,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cI may be treading\r\non dangerous ground; but what I feel about poets and artists in general\r\nis this: on your own lines, you can\xe2\x80\x99t be beaten\xe2\x80\x94granted; but off your\r\nown lines\xe2\x80\x94puff\xe2\x80\x94one has to make allowances. Now, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to\r\nthink that any one had to make allowances for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite agree, Richard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Dalloway. \xe2\x80\x9cThink of Shelley.\r\nI feel that there\xe2\x80\x99s almost everything one wants in \xe2\x80\x98Adonais.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead \xe2\x80\x98Adonais\xe2\x80\x99 by all means,\xe2\x80\x9d Richard conceded. \xe2\x80\x9cBut whenever I hear\r\nof Shelley I repeat to myself the words of Matthew Arnold, \xe2\x80\x98What a set!\r\nWhat a set!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis roused Ridley\xe2\x80\x99s attention. \xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Arnold? A detestable prig!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsnapped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA prig\xe2\x80\x94granted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, I think a man of the world.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s where my point comes in. We politicians doubtless seem to you\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(he grasped somehow that Helen was the representative of the arts) \xe2\x80\x9ca\r\ngross commonplace set of people; but we see both sides; we may be\r\nclumsy, but we do our best to get a grasp of things. Now your artists\r\n_find_ things in a mess, shrug their shoulders, turn aside to their\r\nvisions\xe2\x80\x94which I grant may be very beautiful\xe2\x80\x94and _leave_ things in a\r\nmess. Now that seems to me evading one\xe2\x80\x99s responsibilities. Besides, we\r\naren\xe2\x80\x99t all born with the artistic faculty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s dreadful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Dalloway, who, while her husband spoke, had\r\nbeen thinking. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\xe2\x80\x99m with artists I feel so intensely the delights\r\nof shutting oneself up in a little world of one\xe2\x80\x99s own, with pictures\r\nand music and everything beautiful, and then I go out into the streets\r\nand the first child I meet with its poor, hungry, dirty little face\r\nmakes me turn round and say, \xe2\x80\x98No, I _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ shut myself up\xe2\x80\x94I _won\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\nlive in a world of my own. I should like to stop all the painting and\r\nwriting and music until this kind of thing exists no longer.\xe2\x80\x99 Don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nfeel,\xe2\x80\x9d she wound up, addressing Helen, \xe2\x80\x9cthat life\xe2\x80\x99s a perpetual\r\nconflict?\xe2\x80\x9d Helen considered for a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a pause, which was decidedly uncomfortable. Mrs. Dalloway\r\nthen gave a little shiver, and asked whether she might have her fur\r\ncloak brought to her. As she adjusted the soft brown fur about her neck\r\na fresh topic struck her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI own,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I shall never forget the _Antigone_. I saw it\r\nat Cambridge years ago, and it\xe2\x80\x99s haunted me ever since. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s quite the most modern thing you ever saw?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked Ridley. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nseemed to me I\xe2\x80\x99d known twenty Clytemnestras. Old Lady Ditchling for\r\none. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know a word of Greek, but I could listen to it for ever\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Mr. Pepper struck up:\r\n\r\n\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xe1\xbd\xb0 \xcf\x84\xe1\xbd\xb0 \xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xbd\xce\xac, \xce\xba\xce\xbf\xe1\xbd\x90\xce\xb4\xe1\xbd\xb2\xce\xbd \xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xbd-\r\n\xce\xb8\xcf\x81\xcf\x8e\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xcf\x85 \xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xbd\xcf\x8c\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xce\xbd \xcf\x80\xce\xad\xce\xbb\xce\xb5\xce\xb9.\r\n\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xe1\xbf\xa6\xcf\x84\xce\xbf \xce\xba\xce\xb1\xe1\xbd\xb6 \xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xe1\xbf\xa6 \xcf\x80\xce\xad\xcf\x81\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\r\n\xcf\x80\xcf\x8c\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xcf\x85 \xcf\x87\xce\xb5\xce\xb9\xce\xbc\xce\xb5\xcf\x81\xce\xaf\xe1\xbf\xb3 \xce\xbd\xcf\x8c\xcf\x84\xe1\xbf\xb3\r\n\xcf\x87\xcf\x89\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xe1\xbf\x96, \xcf\x80\xce\xb5\xcf\x81\xce\xb9\xce\xb2\xcf\x81\xcf\x85\xcf\x87\xce\xaf\xce\xbf\xce\xb9\xcf\x83\xce\xb9\r\n\xcf\x80\xce\xb5\xcf\x81\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd \xe1\xbd\x91\xcf\x80\xe1\xbe\xbd \xce\xbf\xe1\xbc\xb4\xce\xb4\xce\xbc\xce\xb1\xcf\x83\xce\xb9.\r\n\r\n\r\nMrs. Dalloway looked at him with compressed lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d give ten years of my life to know Greek,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, when he had\r\ndone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could teach you the alphabet in half an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d read Homer in a month. I should think it an honour to instruct\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelen, engaged with Mr. Dalloway and the habit, now fallen into\r\ndecline, of quoting Greek in the House of Commons, noted, in the great\r\ncommonplace book that lies open beside us as we talk, the fact that all\r\nmen, even men like Ridley, really prefer women to be fashionable.\r\n\r\nClarissa exclaimed that she could think of nothing more delightful. For\r\nan instant she saw herself in her drawing-room in Browne Street with a\r\nPlato open on her knees\xe2\x80\x94Plato in the original Greek. She could not help\r\nbelieving that a real scholar, if specially interested, could slip\r\nGreek into her head with scarcely any trouble.\r\n\r\nRidley engaged her to come to-morrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf only your ship is going to treat us kindly!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, drawing\r\nWilloughby into play. For the sake of guests, and these were\r\ndistinguished, Willoughby was ready with a bow of his head to vouch for\r\nthe good behaviour even of the waves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m dreadfully bad; and my husband\xe2\x80\x99s not very good,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Clarissa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am never sick,\xe2\x80\x9d Richard explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least, I have only been\r\nactually sick once,\xe2\x80\x9d he corrected himself. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was crossing the\r\nChannel. But a choppy sea, I confess, or still worse, a swell, makes me\r\ndistinctly uncomfortable. The great thing is never to miss a meal. You\r\nlook at the food, and you say, \xe2\x80\x98I can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x99; you take a mouthful, and Lord\r\nknows how you\xe2\x80\x99re going to swallow it; but persevere, and you often\r\nsettle the attack for good. My wife\xe2\x80\x99s a coward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were pushing back their chairs. The ladies were hesitating at the\r\ndoorway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d better show the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, advancing.\r\n\r\nRachel followed. She had taken no part in the talk; no one had spoken\r\nto her; but she had listened to every word that was said. She had\r\nlooked from Mrs. Dalloway to Mr. Dalloway, and from Mr. Dalloway back\r\nagain. Clarissa, indeed, was a fascinating spectacle. She wore a white\r\ndress and a long glittering necklace. What with her clothes, and her\r\narch delicate face, which showed exquisitely pink beneath hair turning\r\ngrey, she was astonishingly like an eighteenth-century masterpiece\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nReynolds or a Romney. She made Helen and the others look coarse and\r\nslovenly beside her. Sitting lightly upright she seemed to be dealing\r\nwith the world as she chose; the enormous solid globe spun round this\r\nway and that beneath her fingers. And her husband! Mr. Dalloway rolling\r\nthat rich deliberate voice was even more impressive. He seemed to come\r\nfrom the humming oily centre of the machine where the polished rods are\r\nsliding, and the pistons thumping; he grasped things so firmly but so\r\nloosely; he made the others appear like old maids cheapening remnants.\r\nRachel followed in the wake of the matrons, as if in a trance; a\r\ncurious scent of violets came back from Mrs. Dalloway, mingling with\r\nthe soft rustling of her skirts, and the tinkling of her chains. As she\r\nfollowed, Rachel thought with supreme self-abasement, taking in the\r\nwhole course of her life and the lives of all her friends, \xe2\x80\x9cShe said we\r\nlived in a world of our own. It\xe2\x80\x99s true. We\xe2\x80\x99re perfectly absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe sit in here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, opening the door of the saloon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou play?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Dalloway to Mrs. Ambrose, taking up the score of\r\n_Tristan_ which lay on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy niece does,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, laying her hand on Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how I envy you!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa addressed Rachel for the first time.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you remember this? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it divine?\xe2\x80\x9d She played a bar or two with\r\nringed fingers upon the page.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then Tristan goes like this, and Isolde\xe2\x80\x94oh!\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s all too\r\nthrilling! Have you been to Bayreuth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen that\xe2\x80\x99s still to come. I shall never forget my first _Parsifal_\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ngrilling August day, and those fat old German women, come in their\r\nstuffy high frocks, and then the dark theatre, and the music beginning,\r\nand one couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help sobbing. It caught me here\xe2\x80\x9d (she\r\ntouched her throat). \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s like nothing else in the world! But where\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyour piano?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s in another room,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you will play to us?\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa entreated. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine\r\nanything nicer than to sit out in the moonlight and listen to\r\nmusic\xe2\x80\x94only that sounds too like a schoolgirl! You know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nturning to Helen, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think music\xe2\x80\x99s altogether good for people\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo great a strain?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo emotional, somehow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cOne notices it at once when a\r\nboy or girl takes up music as a profession. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you hate the kind of attitudes people go\r\ninto over Wagner\xe2\x80\x94like this\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She cast her eyes to the ceiling, clasped\r\nher hands, and assumed a look of intensity. \xe2\x80\x9cIt really doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean\r\nthat they appreciate him; in fact, I always think it\xe2\x80\x99s the other way\r\nround. The people who really care about an art are always the least\r\naffected. D\xe2\x80\x99you know Henry Philips, the painter?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo look at, one might think he was a successful stockbroker, and not\r\none of the greatest painters of the age. She could find\r\nno answer, but a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, turning to Rachel, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall insist upon your\r\nplaying to me to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was that in her manner that made Rachel love her.\r\n\r\nMrs. Dalloway hid a tiny yawn, a mere dilation of the nostrils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m extraordinarily sleepy. Instead of letting her\r\nmouth droop, dropping all her clothes in a bunch as though they\r\ndepended on one string, and stretching her limbs to the utmost end of\r\nher berth, she merely changed her dress for a dressing-gown, with\r\ninnumerable frills, and wrapping her feet in a rug, sat down with a\r\nwriting-pad on her knee. Already this cramped little cabin was the\r\ndressing room of a lady of quality. There were bottles containing\r\nliquids; there were trays, boxes, brushes, pins. Evidently not an inch\r\nof her person lacked its proper instrument. The scent which had\r\nintoxicated Rachel pervaded the air. Thus established, Mrs. Dalloway\r\nbegan to write. A pen in her hands became a thing one caressed paper\r\nwith, and she might have been stroking and tickling a kitten as she\r\nwrote:\r\n\r\nPicture us, my dear, afloat in the very oddest ship you can imagine.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s not the ship, so much as the people. One does come across queer\r\nsorts as one travels. I must say I find it hugely amusing. There\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmanager of the line\xe2\x80\x94called Vinrace\xe2\x80\x94a nice big Englishman, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nmuch\xe2\x80\x94you know the sort. As for the rest\xe2\x80\x94they might have come trailing\r\nout of an old number of _Punch_. They\xe2\x80\x99re like people playing croquet in\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x99sixties. It\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99ve always said about\r\nliterary people\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re far the hardest of any to get on with. The\r\nworst of it is, these people\xe2\x80\x94a man and his wife and a niece\xe2\x80\x94might have\r\nbeen, one feels, just like everybody else, if they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got swallowed\r\nup by Oxford or Cambridge or some such place, and been made cranks of.\r\nThe man\xe2\x80\x99s really delightful (if he\xe2\x80\x99d cut his nails), and the woman has\r\nquite a fine face, only she dresses, of course, in a potato sack, and\r\nwears her hair like a Liberty shopgirl\xe2\x80\x99s. They talk about art, and\r\nthink us such poops for dressing in the evening. However, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nthat; I\xe2\x80\x99d rather die than come in to dinner without changing\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou? It matters ever so much more than the soup. (It\xe2\x80\x99s odd how things\r\nlike that _do_ matter so much more than what\xe2\x80\x99s generally supposed to\r\nmatter. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather have my head cut off than wear flannel next the\r\nskin.) Then there\xe2\x80\x99s a nice shy girl\xe2\x80\x94poor thing\xe2\x80\x94I wish one could rake\r\nher out before it\xe2\x80\x99s too late. We ought to start a society for\r\nbroadening the minds of the young\xe2\x80\x94much more useful than missionaries,\r\nHester! Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99d forgotten there\xe2\x80\x99s a dreadful little thing called\r\nPepper. He\xe2\x80\x99s just like his name. He\xe2\x80\x99s indescribably insignificant, and\r\nrather queer in his temper, poor dear. It\xe2\x80\x99s like sitting down to dinner\r\nwith an ill-conditioned fox-terrier, only one can\xe2\x80\x99t comb him out, and\r\nsprinkle him with powder, as one would one\xe2\x80\x99s dog. The great comfort is that\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re away from newspapers, so that Richard will have a real holiday\r\nthis time. Spain wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a holiday. . . .\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou coward!\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard, almost filling the room with his sturdy\r\nfigure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did my duty at dinner!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clarissa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve let yourself in for the Greek alphabet, anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear! Who _is_ Ambrose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gather that he was a Cambridge don; lives in London now, and edits\r\nclassics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever see such a set of cranks? The woman asked me if I thought\r\nher husband looked like a gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was hard to keep the ball rolling at dinner, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRichard. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it that the women, in that class, are so much queerer\r\nthan the men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re not half bad-looking, really\xe2\x80\x94only\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re so odd!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey both laughed, thinking of the same things, so that there was no\r\nneed to compare their impressions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see I shall have quite a lot to say to Vinrace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nknows Sutton and all that set. A boy and girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA pang of envy shot through Mrs. Dalloway\xe2\x80\x99s heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe _must_ have a son, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord, what opportunities there are now for young men!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDalloway, for his talk had set him thinking. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen so good an opening since the days of Pitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it\xe2\x80\x99s yours!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be a leader of men,\xe2\x80\x9d Richard soliloquised. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a fine career. My\r\nGod\xe2\x80\x94what a career!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe chest slowly curved beneath his waistcoat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you know, Dick, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help thinking of England,\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife\r\nmeditatively, leaning her head against his chest. \xe2\x80\x9cBeing on this ship\r\nseems to make it so much more vivid\xe2\x80\x94what it really means to be English.\r\nOne thinks of all we\xe2\x80\x99ve done, and our navies, and the people in India\r\nand Africa, and how we\xe2\x80\x99ve gone on century after century, sending out\r\nboys from little country villages\xe2\x80\x94and of men like you, Dick, and it\r\nmakes one feel as if one couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear _not_ to be English! He ran his mind along the\r\nline of conservative policy, which went steadily from Lord Salisbury to\r\nAlfred, and gradually enclosed, as though it were a lasso that opened\r\nand caught things, enormous chunks of the habitable globe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s taken a long time, but we\xe2\x80\x99ve pretty nearly done it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nremains to consolidate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd these people don\xe2\x80\x99t see it!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt takes all sorts to make a world,\xe2\x80\x9d said her husband. \xe2\x80\x9cThere would\r\nnever be a government if there weren\xe2\x80\x99t an opposition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick, you\xe2\x80\x99re better than I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see round, where I\r\nonly see _there_.\xe2\x80\x9d She pressed a point on the back of his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my business, as I tried to explain at dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I like about you, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, \xe2\x80\x9cis that you\xe2\x80\x99re always\r\nthe same, and I\xe2\x80\x99m a creature of moods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a pretty creature, anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, gazing at her with deeper\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think so, do you? Then kiss me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe kissed her passionately, so that her half-written letter slid to the\r\nground. Picking it up, he read it without asking leave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s your pen?\xe2\x80\x9d he said; and added in his little masculine hand:\r\n\r\nR.D. _loquitur_: Clarice has omitted to tell you that she looked\r\nexceedingly pretty at dinner, and made a conquest by which she has\r\nbound herself to learn the Greek alphabet. I will take this occasion of\r\nadding that we are both enjoying ourselves in these outlandish parts,\r\nand only wish for the presence of our friends (yourself and John, to\r\nwit) to make the trip perfectly enjoyable as it promises to be\r\ninstructive. . . .\r\n\r\n\r\nVoices were heard at the end of the corridor. Mrs. Ambrose was speaking\r\nlow; William Pepper was remarking in his definite and rather acid\r\nvoice, \xe2\x80\x9cThat is the type of lady with whom I find myself distinctly out\r\nof sympathy. She\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut neither Richard nor Clarissa profited by the verdict, for directly\r\nit seemed likely that they would overhear, Richard crackled a sheet of\r\npaper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI often wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa mused in bed, over the little white volume\r\nof Pascal which went with her everywhere, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether it is really good\r\nfor a woman to live with a man who is morally her superior, as Richard\r\nis mine. It makes one so dependent. I suppose I feel for him what my\r\nmother and women of her generation felt for Christ. It just shows that\r\none can\xe2\x80\x99t do without _something_.\xe2\x80\x9d She then fell into a sleep, which\r\nwas as usual extremely sound and refreshing, but visited by fantastic\r\ndreams of great Greek letters stalking round the room, when she woke up\r\nand laughed to herself, remembering where she was and that the Greek\r\nletters were real people, lying asleep not many yards away. Then,\r\nthinking of the black sea outside tossing beneath the moon, she\r\nshuddered, and thought of her husband and the others as companions on\r\nthe voyage. The dreams were not confined to her indeed, but went from\r\none brain to another. They all dreamt of each other that night, as was\r\nnatural, considering how thin the partitions were between them, and how\r\nstrangely they had been lifted off the earth to sit next each other in\r\nmid-ocean, and see every detail of each other\xe2\x80\x99s faces, and hear\r\nwhatever they chanced to say.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nNext morning Clarissa was up before anyone else. She dressed, and was\r\nout on deck, breathing the fresh air of a calm morning, and, making the\r\ncircuit of the ship for the second time, she ran straight into the lean\r\nperson of Mr. Grice, the steward. She apologised, and at the same time\r\nasked him to enlighten her: what were those shiny brass stands for,\r\nhalf glass on the top? She had been wondering, and could not guess.\r\nWhen he had done explaining, she cried enthusiastically:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do think that to be a sailor must be the finest thing in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what d\xe2\x80\x99you know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grice, kindling in a strange\r\nmanner. \xe2\x80\x9cPardon me. What does any man or woman brought up in England\r\nknow about the sea? They profess to know; but they don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bitterness with which he spoke was ominous of what was to come. He\r\nled her off to his own quarters, and, sitting on the edge of a\r\nbrass-bound table, looking uncommonly like a sea-gull, with her white\r\ntapering body and thin alert face, Mrs. Dalloway had to listen to the\r\ntirade of a fanatical man. Did she realise, to begin with, what a very\r\nsmall part of the world the land was? How peaceful, how beautiful, how\r\nbenignant in comparison the sea? The deep waters could sustain Europe\r\nunaided if every earthly animal died of the plague to-morrow. Mr. Grice\r\nrecalled dreadful sights which he had seen in the richest city of the\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x94men and women standing in line hour after hour to receive a mug\r\nof greasy soup. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I thought of the good flesh down here waiting and\r\nasking to be caught. I\xe2\x80\x99m not exactly a Protestant, and I\xe2\x80\x99m not a\r\nCatholic, but I could almost pray for the days of popery to come\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94because of the fasts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he talked he kept opening drawers and moving little glass jars. Here\r\nwere the treasures which the great ocean had bestowed upon him\xe2\x80\x94pale\r\nfish in greenish liquids, blobs of jelly with streaming tresses, fish\r\nwith lights in their heads, they lived so deep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have swum about among bones,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re thinking of Shakespeare,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grice, and taking down a\r\ncopy from a shelf well lined with books, recited in an emphatic nasal\r\nvoice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFull fathom five thy father lies,\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA grand fellow, Shakespeare,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, replacing the volume.\r\n\r\nClarissa was so glad to hear him say so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is your favourite play? I wonder if it\xe2\x80\x99s the same as mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Henry the Fifth_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoy!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_Hamlet_ was what you might call too introspective for Mr. Grice, the\r\nsonnets too passionate; Henry the Fifth was to him the model of an\r\nEnglish gentleman. But his favourite reading was Huxley, Herbert\r\nSpencer, and Henry George; while Emerson and Thomas Hardy he read for\r\nrelaxation. He was giving Mrs. Dalloway his views upon the present\r\nstate of England when the breakfast bell rung so imperiously that she\r\nhad to tear herself away, promising to come back and be shown his\r\nsea-weeds.\r\n\r\nThe party, which had seemed so odd to her the night before, was already\r\ngathered round the table, still under the influence of sleep, and\r\ntherefore uncommunicative, but her entrance sent a little flutter like\r\na breath of air through them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had the most interesting talk of my life!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, taking\r\nher seat beside Willoughby. \xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you realise that one of your men is a\r\nphilosopher and a poet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA very interesting fellow\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what I always say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willoughby,\r\ndistinguishing Mr. Grice. \xe2\x80\x9cThough Rachel finds him a bore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a bore when he talks about currents,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel. Her eyes were\r\nfull of sleep, but Mrs. Dalloway still seemed to her wonderful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never met a bore yet!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I should say the world was full of them!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Helen. But her\r\nbeauty, which was radiant in the morning light, took the contrariness\r\nfrom her words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI agree that it\xe2\x80\x99s the worst one can possibly say of any one,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cHow much rather one would be a murderer than a bore!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded, with her usual air of saying something profound. \xe2\x80\x9cOne can fancy\r\nliking a murderer. It\xe2\x80\x99s the same with dogs. Some dogs are awful bores,\r\npoor dears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt happened that Richard was sitting next to Rachel. She was curiously\r\nconscious of his presence and appearance\xe2\x80\x94his well-cut clothes, his\r\ncrackling shirt-front, his cuffs with blue rings round them, and the\r\nsquare-tipped, very clean fingers with the red stone on the little\r\nfinger of the left hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had a dog who was a bore and knew it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, addressing her in\r\ncool, easy tones. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was a Skye terrier, one of those long chaps, with\r\nlittle feet poking out from their hair like\xe2\x80\x94like caterpillars\xe2\x80\x94no, like\r\nsofas I should say. Well, we had another dog at the same time, a black\r\nbrisk animal\xe2\x80\x94a Schipperke, I think, you call them. You can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine a\r\ngreater contrast. The Skye so slow and deliberate, looking up at you\r\nlike some old gentleman in the club, as much as to say, \xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreally mean it, do you?\xe2\x80\x99 and the Schipperke as quick as a knife. I\r\nliked the Skye best, I must confess. There was something pathetic about\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe story seemed to have no climax.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat happened to him?\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a very sad story,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard, lowering his voice and peeling\r\nan apple. \xe2\x80\x9cHe followed my wife in the car one day and got run over by a\r\nbrute of a cyclist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas he killed?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rachel.\r\n\r\nBut Clarissa at her end of the table had overheard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk of it!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a thing I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to think of\r\nto this day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSurely the tears stood in her eyes?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the painful thing about pets,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Dalloway; \xe2\x80\x9cthey die.\r\nThe first sorrow I can remember was for the death of a dormouse. I\r\nregret to say that I sat upon it. Still, that didn\xe2\x80\x99t make one any the\r\nless sorry. Here lies the duck that Samuel Johnson sat on, eh? I was\r\nbig for my age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we had canaries,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9ca pair of ring-doves, a lemur,\r\nand at one time a martin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you live in the country?\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel asked him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe lived in the country for six months of the year. When I say \xe2\x80\x98we\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nmean four sisters, a brother, and myself. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing like coming\r\nof a large family. Sisters particularly are delightful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDick, you were horribly spoilt!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clarissa across the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no. Appreciated,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard.\r\n\r\nRachel had other questions on the tip of her tongue; or rather one\r\nenormous question, which she did not in the least know how to put into\r\nwords. The talk appeared too airy to admit of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease tell me\xe2\x80\x94everything.\xe2\x80\x9d That was what she wanted to say. He had\r\ndrawn apart one little chink and showed astonishing treasures. It\r\nseemed to her incredible that a man like that should be willing to talk\r\nto her. He had sisters and pets, and once lived in the country. She\r\nstirred her tea round and round; the bubbles which swam and clustered\r\nin the cup seemed to her like the union of their minds.\r\n\r\nThe talk meanwhile raced past her, and when Richard suddenly stated in\r\na jocular tone of voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure Miss Vinrace, now, has secret\r\nleanings towards Catholicism,\xe2\x80\x9d she had no idea what to answer, and\r\nHelen could not help laughing at the start she gave.\r\n\r\nHowever, breakfast was over and Mrs. Dalloway was rising. \xe2\x80\x9cI always\r\nthink religion\xe2\x80\x99s like collecting beetles,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, summing up the\r\ndiscussion as she went up the stairs with Helen. \xe2\x80\x9cOne person has a\r\npassion for black beetles; another hasn\xe2\x80\x99t; it\xe2\x80\x99s no good arguing about\r\nit. What\xe2\x80\x99s _your_ black beetle now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s my children,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s different,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa breathed. \xe2\x80\x9cDo tell me. You have a boy,\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t you? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it detestable, leaving them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was as though a blue shadow had fallen across a pool. Their eyes\r\nbecame deeper, and their voices more cordial. Instead of joining them\r\nas they began to pace the deck, Rachel was indignant with the\r\nprosperous matrons, who made her feel outside their world and\r\nmotherless, and turning back, she left them abruptly. She slammed the\r\ndoor of her room, and pulled out her music. It was all old music\xe2\x80\x94Bach\r\nand Beethoven, Mozart and Purcell\xe2\x80\x94the pages yellow, the engraving rough\r\nto the finger. In three minutes she was deep in a very difficult, very\r\nclassical fugue in A, and over her face came a queer remote impersonal\r\nexpression of complete absorption and anxious satisfaction. Now she\r\nstumbled; now she faltered and had to play the same bar twice over; but\r\nan invisible line seemed to string the notes together, from which rose\r\na shape, a building. She was so far absorbed in this work, for it was\r\nreally difficult to find how all these sounds should stand together,\r\nand drew upon the whole of her faculties, that she never heard a knock\r\nat the door. It was burst impulsively open, and Mrs. Dalloway stood in\r\nthe room leaving the door open, so that a strip of the white deck and\r\nof the blue sea appeared through the opening. The shape of the Bach\r\nfugue crashed to the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let me interrupt,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa implored. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard you playing, and\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t resist. I adore Bach!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRachel flushed and fumbled her fingers in her lap. She stood up\r\nawkwardly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too difficult,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you were playing quite splendidly! I ought to have stayed\r\noutside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\nShe slid _Cowper\xe2\x80\x99s Letters_ and _Wuthering Heights_ out of the\r\narm-chair, so that Clarissa was invited to sit there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a dear little room!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking round. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, _Cowper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nLetters_! I\xe2\x80\x99ve never read them. Are they nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather dull,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wrote awfully well, didn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa; \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94if one likes that\r\nkind of thing\xe2\x80\x94finished his sentences and all that. _Wuthering Heights_!\r\nAh\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s more in my line. I really couldn\xe2\x80\x99t exist without the Brontes!\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you love them? Still, on the whole, I\xe2\x80\x99d rather live without them\r\nthan without Jane Austen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLightly and at random though she spoke, her manner conveyed an\r\nextraordinary degree of sympathy and desire to befriend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJane Austen? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like Jane Austen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou monster!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI can only just forgive you. Tell\r\nme why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s so\xe2\x80\x94so\xe2\x80\x94well, so like a tight plait,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel floundered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94I see what you mean. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t agree. And you won\xe2\x80\x99t when you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nolder. At your age I only liked Shelley. I can remember sobbing over\r\nhim in the garden.\r\n\r\nHe has outsoared the shadow of our night,\r\nEnvy and calumny and hate and pain\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\nyou remember?\r\n\r\nCan touch him not and torture not again\r\nFrom the contagion of the world\xe2\x80\x99s slow stain.\r\n\r\n\r\nHow divine!\xe2\x80\x94and yet what nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d She looked lightly round the room.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always think it\xe2\x80\x99s _living_, not dying, that counts. I really respect\r\nsome snuffy old stockbroker who\xe2\x80\x99s gone on adding up column after column\r\nall his days, and trotting back to his villa at Brixton with some old\r\npug dog he worships, and a dreary little wife sitting at the end of the\r\ntable, and going off to Margate for a fortnight\xe2\x80\x94I assure you I know\r\nheaps like that\xe2\x80\x94well, they seem to me _really_ nobler than poets whom\r\nevery one worships, just because they\xe2\x80\x99re geniuses and die young. But I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t expect _you_ to agree with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe pressed Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm-m-m\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she went on quoting\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nUnrest which men miscall delight\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhen you\xe2\x80\x99re my age you\xe2\x80\x99ll see that the world is _crammed_ with\r\ndelightful things. I think young people make such a mistake about\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94not letting themselves be happy. I sometimes think that happiness\r\nis the only thing that counts. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know you well enough to say, but\r\nI should guess you might be a little inclined to\xe2\x80\x94when one\xe2\x80\x99s young and\r\nattractive\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m going to say it!\xe2\x80\x94_every_thing\xe2\x80\x99s at one\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nglanced round as much as to say, \xe2\x80\x9cnot only a few stuffy books and\r\nBach.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI long to ask questions,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued. \xe2\x80\x9cYou interest me so much. If\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m impertinent, you must just box my ears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x94I want to ask questions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel with such earnestness that\r\nMrs. Dalloway had to check her smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you mind if we walk?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe air\xe2\x80\x99s so delicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe snuffed it like a racehorse as they shut the door and stood on\r\ndeck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it good to be alive?\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, and drew Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s arm\r\nwithin hers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook, look! How exquisite!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shores of Portugal were beginning to lose their substance; but the\r\nland was still the land, though at a great distance. They could\r\ndistinguish the little towns that were sprinkled in the folds of the\r\nhills, and the smoke rising faintly. The towns appeared to be very\r\nsmall in comparison with the great purple mountains behind them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonestly, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa, having looked, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like views.\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99re too inhuman.\xe2\x80\x9d They walked on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow odd it is!\xe2\x80\x9d she continued impulsively. \xe2\x80\x9cThis time yesterday we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nnever met. I was packing in a stuffy little room in the hotel. We know\r\nabsolutely nothing about each other\xe2\x80\x94and yet\xe2\x80\x94I feel as if I _did_ know\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have children\xe2\x80\x94your husband was in Parliament?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never been to school, and you live\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith my aunts at Richmond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichmond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, my aunts like the Park. They like the quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you don\xe2\x80\x99t! I understand!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like walking in the Park alone; but not\xe2\x80\x94with the dogs,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nfinished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; and some people _are_ dogs; aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa, as if she\r\nhad guessed a secret. \xe2\x80\x9cBut not every one\xe2\x80\x94oh no, not every one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot every one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel, and stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can quite imagine you walking alone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa: \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nthinking\xe2\x80\x94in a little world of your own. But how you will enjoy it\xe2\x80\x94some\r\nday!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall enjoy walking with a man\xe2\x80\x94is that what you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel,\r\nregarding Mrs. Dalloway with her large enquiring eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t thinking of a man particularly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you\r\nwill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I shall never marry,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel determined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so sure of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. Her sidelong glance\r\ntold Rachel that she found her attractive although she was inexplicably\r\namused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do people marry?\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what you\xe2\x80\x99re going to find out,\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa laughed.\r\n\r\nRachel followed her eyes and found that they rested for a second, on\r\nthe robust figure of Richard Dalloway, who was engaged in striking a\r\nmatch on the sole of his boot; while Willoughby expounded something,\r\nwhich seemed to be of great interest to them both.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing like it,\xe2\x80\x9d she concluded. \xe2\x80\x9cDo tell me about the\r\nAmbroses. Or am I asking too many questions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI find you easy to talk to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\nThe short sketch of the Ambroses was, however, somewhat perfunctory,\r\nand contained little but the fact that Mr. Ambrose was her uncle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother\xe2\x80\x99s brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen a name has dropped out of use, the lightest touch upon it tells.\r\nMrs. Dalloway went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you like your mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; she was different,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\nShe was overcome by an intense desire to tell Mrs. Dalloway things she\r\nhad never told any one\xe2\x80\x94things she had not realised herself until this\r\nmoment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am lonely,\xe2\x80\x9d she began. \xe2\x80\x9cI want\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She did not know what she wanted,\r\nso that she could not finish the sentence; but her lip quivered.\r\n\r\nBut it seemed that Mrs. Dalloway was able to understand without words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, actually putting one arm round Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was your age I wanted too. No one understood until I met\r\nRichard. He gave me all I wanted. He\xe2\x80\x99s man and woman as well.\xe2\x80\x9d Her eyes\r\nrested upon Mr. Dalloway, leaning upon the rail, still talking. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I say that because I\xe2\x80\x99m his wife\xe2\x80\x94I see his faults more clearly\r\nthan I see any one else\xe2\x80\x99s. What one wants in the person one lives with\r\nis that they should keep one at one\xe2\x80\x99s best. I often wonder what I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ndone to be so happy!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, and a tear slid down her cheek.\r\nShe wiped it away, squeezed Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s hand, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow good life is!\xe2\x80\x9d At that moment, standing out in the fresh breeze,\r\nwith the sun upon the waves, and Mrs. Dalloway\xe2\x80\x99s hand upon her arm, it\r\nseemed indeed as if life which had been unnamed before was infinitely\r\nwonderful, and too good to be true.\r\n\r\nHere Helen passed them, and seeing Rachel arm-in-arm with a comparative\r\nstranger, looking excited, was amused, but at the same time slightly\r\nirritated. But they were immediately joined by Richard, who had enjoyed\r\na very interesting talk with Willoughby and was in a sociable mood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObserve my Panama,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, touching the brim of his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you\r\naware, Miss Vinrace, how much can be done to induce fine weather by\r\nappropriate headdress? I have determined that it is a hot summer day; I\r\nwarn you that nothing you can say will shake me. Therefore I am going\r\nto sit down. I advise you to follow my example.\xe2\x80\x9d Three chairs in a row\r\ninvited them to be seated.\r\n\r\nLeaning back, Richard surveyed the waves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a very pretty blue,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s a little too much of\r\nit. Variety is essential to a view. Thus, if you have hills you ought\r\nto have a river; if a river, hills. The best view in the world in my\r\nopinion is that from Boars Hill on a fine day\xe2\x80\x94it must be a fine day,\r\nmark you\xe2\x80\x94A rug?\xe2\x80\x94Oh, thank you, my dear . . . in that case you have also\r\nthe advantage of associations\xe2\x80\x94the Past.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you want to talk, Dick, or shall I read aloud?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarissa had fetched a book with the rugs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Persuasion_,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Richard, examining the volume.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for Miss Vinrace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cShe can\xe2\x80\x99t bear our beloved\r\nJane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x94if I may say so\xe2\x80\x94is because you have not read her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is incomparably the greatest female writer we possess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the greatest,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cand for this reason: she does not\r\nattempt to write like a man. Every other woman does; on that account, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t read \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProduce your instances, Miss Vinrace,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, joining his\r\nfinger-tips. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m ready to be converted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe waited, while Rachel vainly tried to vindicate her sex from the\r\nslight he put upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid he\xe2\x80\x99s right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa. \xe2\x80\x9cHe generally is\xe2\x80\x94the wretch!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI brought _Persuasion_,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I thought it was a\r\nlittle less threadbare than the others\xe2\x80\x94though, Dick, it\xe2\x80\x99s no good\r\n_your_ pretending to know Jane by heart, considering that she always\r\nsends you to sleep!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter the labours of legislation, I deserve sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not to think about those guns,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa, seeing that his\r\neye, passing over the waves, still sought the land meditatively, \xe2\x80\x9cor\r\nabout navies, or empires, or anything.\xe2\x80\x9d So saying she opened the book\r\nand began to read:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Sir Walter Elliott, of Kellynch Hall, in Somersetshire, was a man\r\nwho, for his own amusement, never took up any book but the\r\n_Baronetage_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you know Sir Walter?\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98There he found occupation for\r\nan idle hour, and consolation in a distressed one.\xe2\x80\x99 She does write\r\nwell, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she? \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d She read on in a light humorous voice. She\r\nwas determined that Sir Walter should take her husband\xe2\x80\x99s mind off the\r\nguns of Britain, and divert him in an exquisite, quaint, sprightly, and\r\nslightly ridiculous world. After a time it appeared that the sun was\r\nsinking in that world, and the points becoming softer. Rachel looked up\r\nto see what caused the change. Richard\xe2\x80\x99s eyelids were closing and\r\nopening; opening and closing. A loud nasal breath announced that he no\r\nlonger considered appearances, that he was sound asleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTriumph!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa whispered at the end of a sentence. Suddenly she\r\nraised her hand in protest. A sailor hesitated; she gave the book to\r\nRachel, and stepped lightly to take the message\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grice wished to\r\nknow if it was convenient,\xe2\x80\x9d etc. She followed him. Ridley, who had\r\nprowled unheeded, started forward, stopped, and, with a gesture of\r\ndisgust, strode off to his study. The sleeping politician was left in\r\nRachel\xe2\x80\x99s charge. She read a sentence, and took a look at him. In sleep\r\nhe looked like a coat hanging at the end of a bed; there were all the\r\nwrinkles, and the sleeves and trousers kept their shape though no\r\nlonger filled out by legs and arms. You can then best judge the age and\r\nstate of the coat. She looked him all over until it seemed to her that\r\nhe must protest.\r\n\r\nHe was a man of forty perhaps; and here there were lines round his\r\neyes, and there curious clefts in his cheeks. Slightly battered he\r\nappeared, but dogged and in the prime of life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSisters and a dormouse and some canaries,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel murmured, never\r\ntaking her eyes off him. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder, I wonder.\xe2\x80\x9d She ceased, her chin\r\nupon her hand, still looking at him. A bell chimed behind them, and\r\nRichard raised his head. Then he opened his eyes which wore for a\r\nsecond the queer look of a shortsighted person\xe2\x80\x99s whose spectacles are\r\nlost. It took him a moment to recover from the impropriety of having\r\nsnored, and possibly grunted, before a young lady. To wake and find\r\noneself left alone with one was also slightly disconcerting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I\xe2\x80\x99ve been dozing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s happened to everyone?\r\nClarissa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Dalloway has gone to look at Mr. Grice\xe2\x80\x99s fish,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might have guessed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a common occurrence. And\r\nhow have you improved the shining hour? Have you become a convert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99ve read a line,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I always find. My best ideas have come to me out\r\nof doors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you were walking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalking\xe2\x80\x94riding\xe2\x80\x94yachting\xe2\x80\x94I suppose the most momentous conversations of\r\nmy life took place while perambulating the great court at Trinity. I\r\nwas at both universities. It was a fad of my father\xe2\x80\x99s. He thought it\r\nbroadening to the mind. I think I agree with him. I can remember\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nan age ago it seems!\xe2\x80\x94settling the basis of a future state with the\r\npresent Secretary for India. We thought ourselves very wise. I answer\xe2\x80\x94Yes and No. If on the one hand I have\r\nnot accomplished what I set out to accomplish\xe2\x80\x94which of us does!\xe2\x80\x94on the\r\nother I can fairly say this: I have not lowered my ideal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked resolutely at a sea-gull, as though his ideal flew on the\r\nwings of the bird.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat _is_ your ideal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you ask too much, Miss Vinrace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard playfully.\r\n\r\nShe could only say that she wanted to know, and Richard was\r\nsufficiently amused to answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how shall I reply? In one word\xe2\x80\x94Unity. Unity of aim, of dominion,\r\nof progress. The dispersion of the best ideas over the greatest area.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI grant that the English seem, on the whole, whiter than most men,\r\ntheir records cleaner. I\xe2\x80\x99m under no illusions. Few people, I suppose, have fewer\r\nillusions than I have. Have you ever been in a factory, Miss\r\nVinrace!\xe2\x80\x94No, I suppose not\xe2\x80\x94I may say I hope not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs for Rachel, she had scarcely walked through a poor street, and\r\nalways under the escort of father, maid, or aunts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was going to say that if you\xe2\x80\x99d ever seen the kind of thing that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing on round you, you\xe2\x80\x99d understand what it is that makes me and men\r\nlike me politicians. She liked Richard Dalloway, and warmed as he warmed. He seemed\r\nto mean what he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s far better that you should know nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said paternally,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you wrong yourself, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure. It is impossible for human beings, constituted as they are,\r\nboth to fight and to have ideals. If I have preserved mine, as I am\r\nthankful to say that in great measure I have, it is due to the fact\r\nthat I have been able to come home to my wife in the evening and to\r\nfind that she has spent her day in calling, music, play with the\r\nchildren, domestic duties\xe2\x80\x94what you will; her illusions have not been\r\ndestroyed. She gives me courage to go on. The strain of public life is\r\nvery great,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\nThis made him appear a battered martyr, parting every day with some of\r\nthe finest gold, in the service of mankind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9chow any one does it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain, Miss Vinrace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a matter I want to\r\nclear up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis kindness was genuine, and she determined to take the chance he gave\r\nher, although to talk to a man of such worth and authority made her\r\nheart beat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me like this,\xe2\x80\x9d she began, doing her best first to\r\nrecollect and then to expose her shivering private visions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s an old widow in her room, somewhere, let us suppose in the\r\nsuburbs of Leeds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRichard bent his head to show that he accepted the widow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn London you\xe2\x80\x99re spending your life, talking, writing things, getting\r\nbills through, missing what seems natural. The result of it all is that\r\nshe goes to her cupboard and finds a little more tea, a few lumps of\r\nsugar, or a little less tea and a newspaper. Widows all over the\r\ncountry I admit do this. Still, there\xe2\x80\x99s the mind of the widow\xe2\x80\x94the\r\naffections; those you leave untouched. But you waste you own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the widow goes to her cupboard and finds it bare,\xe2\x80\x9d Richard\r\nanswered, \xe2\x80\x9cher spiritual outlook we may admit will be affected. If I\r\nmay pick holes in your philosophy, Miss Vinrace, which has its merits,\r\nI would point out that a human being is not a set of compartments, but\r\nan organism. Imagination, Miss Vinrace; use your imagination; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhere you young Liberals fail. Conceive the world as a whole. Now for\r\nyour second point; when you assert that in trying to set the house in\r\norder for the benefit of the young generation I am wasting my higher\r\ncapabilities, I totally disagree with you. I can conceive no more\r\nexalted aim\xe2\x80\x94to be the citizen of the Empire. Look at it in this way,\r\nMiss Vinrace; conceive the state as a complicated machine; we citizens\r\nare parts of that machine; some fulfil more important duties; others\r\n(perhaps I am one of them) serve only to connect some obscure parts of\r\nthe mechanism, concealed from the public eye. Yet if the meanest screw\r\nfails in its task, the proper working of the whole is imperilled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was impossible to combine the image of a lean black widow, gazing\r\nout of her window, and longing for some one to talk to, with the image\r\nof a vast machine, such as one sees at South Kensington, thumping,\r\nthumping, thumping. The attempt at communication had been a failure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to understand each other,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I say something that will make you very angry?\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then; no woman has what I may call the political instinct. You\r\nhave very great virtues; I am the first, I hope, to admit that; but I\r\nhave never met a woman who even saw what is meant by statesmanship. I\r\nam going to make you still more angry. I hope that I never shall meet\r\nsuch a woman. In things like\r\ndust-carts, and men mending roads? You feel that all the time when you\r\nwalk about London, and when you turn on a tap and the water comes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cI understand you to mean that the whole of\r\nmodern society is based upon cooperative effort. If only more people\r\nwould realise that, Miss Vinrace, there would be fewer of your old\r\nwidows in solitary lodgings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRachel considered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you a Liberal or are you a Conservative?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI call myself a Conservative for convenience sake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard,\r\nsmiling. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there is more in common between the two parties than\r\npeople generally allow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a pause, which did not come on Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s side from any lack of\r\nthings to say; as usual she could not say them, and was further\r\nconfused by the fact that the time for talking probably ran short. She\r\nwas haunted by absurd jumbled ideas\xe2\x80\x94how, if one went back far enough,\r\neverything perhaps was intelligible; everything was in common; for the\r\nmammoths who pastured in the fields of Richmond High Street had turned\r\ninto paving stones and boxes full of ribbon, and her aunts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you say you lived in the country when you were a child?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\nCrude as her manners seemed to him, Richard was flattered. There could\r\nbe no doubt that her interest was genuine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did,\xe2\x80\x9d he smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what happened?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cOr do I ask too many questions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m flattered, I assure you. But\xe2\x80\x94let me see\xe2\x80\x94what happened? Well,\r\nriding, lessons, sisters. There was an enchanted rubbish heap, I\r\nremember, where all kinds of queer things happened. Odd, what things\r\nimpress children! I can remember the look of the place to this day.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a fallacy to think that children are happy. They\xe2\x80\x99re not; they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nunhappy. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never suffered so much as I did when I was a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t get on well with my father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was a\r\nvery able man, but hard. Well\xe2\x80\x94it makes one determined not to sin in\r\nthat way oneself. Children never forget injustice. They forgive heaps\r\nof things grown-up people mind; but that sin is the unpardonable sin.\r\nMind you\xe2\x80\x94I daresay I was a difficult child to manage; but when I think\r\nwhat I was ready to give! No, I was more sinned against than sinning.\r\nAnd then I went to school, where I did very fairly well; and and then,\r\nas I say, my father sent me to both universities. . . . D\xe2\x80\x99you know,\r\nMiss Vinrace, you\xe2\x80\x99ve made me think? How little, after all, one can tell\r\nanybody about one\xe2\x80\x99s life! Here I sit; there you sit; both, I doubt not,\r\nchock-full of the most interesting experiences, ideas, emotions; yet\r\nhow communicate? I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you what every second person you meet might\r\ntell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the way of saying things, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it,\r\nnot the things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly true.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I look back\r\nover my life\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m forty-two\xe2\x80\x94what are the great facts that stand out?\r\nWhat were the revelations, if I may call them so? The misery of the\r\npoor and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (he hesitated and pitched over) \xe2\x80\x9clove!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUpon that word he lowered his voice; it was a word that seemed to\r\nunveil the skies for Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an odd thing to say to a young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cBut have you\r\nany idea what\xe2\x80\x94what I mean by that? No, of course not. I don\xe2\x80\x99t use the\r\nword in a conventional sense. I use it as young men use it. Girls are\r\nkept very ignorant, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they? Perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s wise\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94You _don\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\nknow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke as if he had lost consciousness of what he was saying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, scarcely speaking above her breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWarships, Dick! Over there! Look!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa, released from Mr. Grice,\r\nappreciative of all his seaweeds, skimmed towards them, gesticulating.\r\n\r\nShe had sighted two sinister grey vessels, low in the water, and bald\r\nas bone, one closely following the other with the look of eyeless\r\nbeasts seeking their prey. Consciousness returned to Richard instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, and stood shielding his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOurs, Dick?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clarissa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Mediterranean Fleet,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\nThe _Euphrosyne_ was slowly dipping her flag. Richard raised his hat.\r\nConvulsively Clarissa squeezed Rachel\xe2\x80\x99s hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you glad to be English!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nThe warships drew past, casting a curious effect of discipline and\r\nsadness upon the waters, and it was not until they were again invisible\r\nthat people spoke to each other naturally. At lunch the talk was all of\r\nvalour and death, and the magnificent qualities of British admirals.\r\nClarissa quoted one poet, Willoughby quoted another. Life on board a\r\nman-of-war was splendid, so they agreed, and sailors, whenever one met\r\nthem, were quite especially nice and simple.\r\n\r\nThis being so, no one liked it when Helen remarked that it seemed to\r\nher as wrong to keep sailors as to keep a Zoo, and that as for dying on\r\na battle-field, surely it was time we ceased to praise courage\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cor to\r\nwrite bad poetry about it,\xe2\x80\x9d snarled Pepper.\r\n\r\nBut Helen was really wondering why Rachel, sitting silent, looked so\r\nqueer and flushed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nShe was not able to follow up her observations, however, or to come to\r\nany conclusion, for by one of those accidents which are liable to\r\nhappen at sea, the whole course of their lives was now put out of\r\norder.\r\n\r\nEven at tea the floor rose beneath their feet and pitched too low\r\nagain, and at dinner the ship seemed to groan and strain as though a\r\nlash were descending. She who had been a broad-backed dray-horse, upon\r\nwhose hind-quarters pierrots might waltz, became a colt in a field. The\r\nplates slanted away from the knives, and Mrs. Dalloway\xe2\x80\x99s face blanched\r\nfor a second as she helped herself and saw the potatoes roll this way\r\nand that. Willoughby, of course, extolled the virtues of his ship, and\r\nquoted what had been said of her by experts and distinguished\r\npassengers, for he loved his own possessions. Still, dinner was uneasy,\r\nand directly the ladies were alone Clarissa owned that she would be\r\nbetter off in bed, and went, smiling bravely.\r\n\r\nNext morning the storm was on them, and no politeness could ignore it.\r\nMrs. Dalloway stayed in her room. Richard faced three meals, eating\r\nvaliantly at each; but at the third, certain glazed asparagus swimming\r\nin oil finally conquered him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat beats me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and withdrew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we are alone once more,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked William Pepper, looking round\r\nthe table; but no one was ready to engage him in talk, and the meal\r\nended in silence.\r\n\r\nOn the following day they met\xe2\x80\x94but as flying leaves meet in the air.\r\nSick they were not; but the wind propelled them hastily into rooms,\r\nviolently downstairs. They passed each other gasping on deck; they\r\nshouted across tables. They wore fur coats; and Helen was never seen\r\nwithout a bandanna on her head. For comfort they retreated to their\r\ncabins, where with tightly wedged feet they let the ship bounce and\r\ntumble. Their sensations were the sensations of potatoes in a sack on a\r\ngalloping horse. The world outside was merely a violent grey tumult.\r\nFor two days they had a perfect rest from their old emotions. Rachel\r\nhad just enough consciousness to suppose herself a donkey on the summit\r\nof a moor in a hail-storm, with its coat blown into furrows; then she\r\nbecame a wizened tree, perpetually driven back by the salt Atlantic\r\ngale.\r\n\r\nHelen, on the other hand, staggered to Mrs. Dalloway\xe2\x80\x99s door, knocked,\r\ncould not be heard for the slamming of doors and the battering of wind,\r\nand entered.\r\n\r\nThere were basins, of course. Mrs. Dalloway lay half-raised on a\r\npillow, and did not open her eyes. Then she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dick, is\r\nthat you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelen shouted\xe2\x80\x94for she was thrown against the washstand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClarissa opened one eye. It gave her an incredibly dissipated\r\nappearance. \xe2\x80\x9cAwful!\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped. Her lips were white inside.\r\n\r\nPlanting her feet wide, Helen contrived to pour champagne into a\r\ntumbler with a tooth-brush in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChampagne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a tooth-brush in it,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Clarissa, and smiled; it might\r\nhave been the contortion of one weeping. She drank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDisgusting,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, indicating the basins. Relics of humour\r\nstill played over her face like moonshine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWant more?\xe2\x80\x9d Helen shouted. Speech was again beyond Clarissa\xe2\x80\x99s reach.\r\nThe wind laid the ship shivering on her side. Pale agonies crossed Mrs.\r\nDalloway in waves. When the curtains flapped, grey lights puffed across\r\nher. Between the spasms of the storm, Helen made the curtain fast,\r\nshook the pillows, stretched the bed-clothes, and smoothed the hot\r\nnostrils and forehead with cold scent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _are_ good!\xe2\x80\x9d Clarissa gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cHorrid mess!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was trying to apologise for white underclothes fallen and scattered\r\non the floor. For one second she opened a single eye, and saw that the\r\nroom was tidy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped.\r\n\r\nHelen left her; far, far away she knew that she felt a kind of liking\r\nfor Mrs. Dalloway. She could not help respecting her spirit and her\r\ndesire, even in the throes of sickness, for a tidy bedroom. Her\r\npetticoats, however, rose above her knees.\r\n\r\nQuite suddenly the storm relaxed its grasp. It happened at tea; the\r\nexpected paroxysm of the blast gave out just as it reached its climax\r\nand dwindled away, and the ship instead of taking the usual plunge went\r\nsteadily. The monotonous order of plunging and rising, roaring and\r\nrelaxing, was interfered with, and every one at table looked up and\r\nfelt something loosen within them. The strain was slackened and human\r\nfeelings began to peep again, as they do when daylight shows at the end\r\nof a tunnel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry a turn with me,\xe2\x80\x9d Ridley called across to Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoolish!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Helen, but they went stumbling up the ladder. Choked\r\nby the wind their spirits rose with a rush, for on the skirts of all\r\nthe grey tumult was a misty spot of gold. Instantly the world dropped\r\ninto shape; they were no longer atoms flying in the void, but people\r\nriding a triumphant ship on the back of the sea. Wind and space were\r\nbanished; the world floated like an apple in a tub, and the mind of\r\nman, which had been unmoored also, once more attached itself to the old\r\nbeliefs.\r\n\r\nHaving scrambled twice round the ship and received many sound cuffs\r\nfrom the wind, they saw a sailor\xe2\x80\x99s face positively shine golden. They\r\nlooked, and beheld a complete yellow circle of sun; next minute it was\r\ntraversed by sailing stands of cloud, and then completely hidden. By\r\nbreakfast the next morning, however, the sky was swept clean, the\r\nwaves, although steep, were blue, and after their view of the strange\r\nunder-world, inhabited by phantoms, people began to live among tea-pots\r\nand loaves of bread with greater zest than ever.\r\n\r\nRichard and Clarissa, however, still remained on the borderland. She\r\ndid not attempt to sit up; her husband stood on his feet, contemplated\r\nhis waistcoat and trousers, shook his head, and then lay down again.\r\nThe inside of his brain was still rising and falling like the sea on\r\nthe stage. At four o\xe2\x80\x99clock he woke from sleep and saw the sunlight make\r\na vivid angle across the red plush curtains and the grey tweed\r\ntrousers. The ordinary world outside slid into his mind, and by the\r\ntime he was dressed he was an English gentleman again.\r\n\r\nHe stood beside his wife. She pulled him down to her by the lapel of\r\nhis coat, kissed him, and held him fast for a minute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and get a breath of air, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou look quite washed\r\nout. . . . How nice you smell! . . . And be polite to that woman. She\r\nwas so kind to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThereupon Mrs. Dalloway turned to the cool side of her pillow, terribly\r\nflattened but still invincible.\r\n\r\nRichard found Helen talking to her brother-in-law, over two dishes of\r\nyellow cake and smooth bread and butter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look very ill!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed on seeing him. \xe2\x80\x9cCome and have some\r\ntea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe remarked that the hands that moved about the cups were beautiful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear you\xe2\x80\x99ve been very good to my wife,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s had an awful\r\ntime of it. You came in and fed her with champagne. Were you among the\r\nsaved yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? Oh, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been sick for twenty years\xe2\x80\x94sea-sick, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are three stages of convalescence, I always say,\xe2\x80\x9d broke in the\r\nhearty voice of Willoughby. \xe2\x80\x9cThe milk stage, the bread-and-butter\r\nstage, and the roast-beef stage. I should say you were at the\r\nbread-and-butter stage.\xe2\x80\x9d He handed him the plate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, I should advise a hearty tea, then a brisk walk on deck; and by\r\ndinner-time you\xe2\x80\x99ll be clamouring for beef, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d He went off laughing,\r\nexcusing himself on the score of business.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a splendid fellow he is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cAlways keen on\r\nsomething.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s always been like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a great undertaking of his,\xe2\x80\x9d Richard continued. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbusiness that won\xe2\x80\x99t stop with ships, I should say. We shall see him in\r\nParliament, or I\xe2\x80\x99m much mistaken. He\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of man we want in\r\nParliament\xe2\x80\x94the man who has done things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Helen was not much interested in her brother-in-law.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect your head\xe2\x80\x99s aching, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, pouring a fresh\r\ncup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s humiliating to find what a slave one\r\nis to one\xe2\x80\x99s body in this world. D\xe2\x80\x99you know, I can never work without a\r\nkettle on the hob. As often as not I don\xe2\x80\x99t drink tea, but I must feel\r\nthat I can if I want to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s very bad for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt shortens one\xe2\x80\x99s life; but I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, Mrs. Ambrose, we politicians\r\nmust make up our minds to that at the outset. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got to burn the\r\ncandle at both ends, or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve cooked your goose!\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen brightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t make you take us seriously, Mrs. Ambrose,\xe2\x80\x9d he protested. \xe2\x80\x9cMay\r\nI ask how you\xe2\x80\x99ve spent your time? Reading\xe2\x80\x94philosophy?\xe2\x80\x9d (He saw the\r\nblack book.) \xe2\x80\x9cMetaphysics and fishing!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I had to live\r\nagain I believe I should devote myself to one or the other.\xe2\x80\x9d He began\r\nturning the pages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Good, then, is indefinable,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he read out. \xe2\x80\x9cHow jolly to think that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing on still! \xe2\x80\x98So far as I know there is only one ethical writer,\r\nProfessor Henry Sidgwick, who has clearly recognised and stated this\r\nfact.\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s just the kind of thing we used to talk about when we were\r\nboys. I can remember arguing until five in the morning with Duffy\xe2\x80\x94now\r\nSecretary for India\xe2\x80\x94pacing round and round those cloisters until we\r\ndecided it was too late to go to bed, and we went for a ride instead.\r\nWhether we ever came to any conclusion\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s another matter. His body, which had grown white and tender in a dark room,\r\ntingled all over in the fresh air. He felt himself a man undoubtedly in\r\nthe prime of life. Pride glowed in his eye as he let the wind buffet\r\nhim and stood firm. With his head slightly lowered he sheered round\r\ncorners, strode uphill, and met the blast. There was a collision. For a\r\nsecond he could not see what the body was he had run into. \xe2\x80\x9cSorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry.\xe2\x80\x9d It was Rachel who apologised. They both laughed, too much\r\nblown about to speak. She drove open the door of her room and stepped\r\ninto its calm. In order to speak to her, it was necessary that Richard\r\nshould follow. They stood in a whirlpool of wind; papers began flying\r\nround in circles, the door crashed to, and they tumbled, laughing, into\r\nchairs. Richard sat upon Bach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word! What a tempest!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel. Certainly the struggle and wind had\r\ngiven her a decision she lacked; red was in her cheeks, and her hair\r\nwas down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what fun!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I sitting on? Is this your room? How\r\njolly!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94sit there,\xe2\x80\x9d she commanded. Cowper slid once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow jolly to meet again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Richard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems an age. _Cowper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nLetters_? . . . Bach? . . . _Wuthering Heights_? . . . Is this where\r\nyou meditate on the world, and then come out and pose poor politicians\r\nwith questions? In the intervals of sea-sickness I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought a lot of\r\nour talk. I assure you, you made me think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made you think! Well, then I shall make a point of sending you a copy. _The Speech\r\non the French Revolution_\xe2\x80\x94_The American Rebellion_? Which shall it be,\r\nI wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d He noted something in his pocket-book. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then you must\r\nwrite and tell me what you think of it. This reticence\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nisolation\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with modern life! Now, tell me about\r\nyourself. What are your interests and occupations?
