Round and round they go\xe2\x80\x94out there, over\r\nthe rim\xe2\x80\x94out of sight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis fingers showed that the waltzing teetotums had spun over the edge\r\nof the counterpane and fallen off the bed into infinity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you contemplate three weeks alone in this hotel?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hirst,\r\nafter a moment\xe2\x80\x99s pause.\r\n\r\nHewet proceeded to think.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe truth of it is that one never is alone, and one never is in\r\ncompany,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeaning?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeaning? Oh, something about bubbles\xe2\x80\x94auras\xe2\x80\x94what d\xe2\x80\x99you call \xe2\x80\x99em? You\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t see my bubble; I can\xe2\x80\x99t see yours; all we see of each other is a\r\nspeck, like the wick in the middle of that flame. The flame goes about\r\nwith us everywhere; it\xe2\x80\x99s not ourselves exactly, but what we feel; the\r\nworld is short, or people mainly; all kinds of people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice streaky bubble yours must be!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd supposing my bubble could run into some one else\xe2\x80\x99s bubble\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they both burst?\xe2\x80\x9d put in Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen\xe2\x80\x94then\xe2\x80\x94then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d pondered Hewet, as if to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cit would be an\r\ne-nor-mous world,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, stretching his arms to their full width, as\r\nthough even so they could hardly clasp the billowy universe, for when\r\nhe was with Hirst he always felt unusually sanguine and vague.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you altogether as foolish as I used to, Hewet,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nHirst. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean but you try to say it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut aren\xe2\x80\x99t you enjoying yourself here?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hewet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the whole\xe2\x80\x94yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst. \xe2\x80\x9cI like observing people. I like\r\nlooking at things. This country is amazingly beautiful. Did you notice\r\nhow the top of the mountain turned yellow to-night? Really we must take\r\nour lunch and spend the day out. You\xe2\x80\x99re getting disgustingly fat.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\npointed at the calf of Hewet\xe2\x80\x99s bare leg.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll get up an expedition,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet energetically. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll ask the\r\nentire hotel. We\xe2\x80\x99ll hire donkeys and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst, \xe2\x80\x9cdo shut it! I can see Miss Warrington and Miss\r\nAllan and Mrs. Elliot and the rest squatting on the stones and\r\nquacking, \xe2\x80\x98How jolly!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll ask Venning and Perrott and Miss Murgatroyd\xe2\x80\x94every one we can lay\r\nhands on,\xe2\x80\x9d went on Hewet. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the name of the little old\r\ngrasshopper with the eyeglasses? Pepper?\xe2\x80\x94Pepper shall lead us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God, you\xe2\x80\x99ll never get the donkeys,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must make a note of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet, slowly dropping his feet to\r\nthe floor. \xe2\x80\x9cHirst escorts Miss Warrington; Pepper advances alone on a\r\nwhite ass; provisions equally distributed\xe2\x80\x94or shall we hire a mule? The\r\nmatrons\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s Mrs. Paley, by Jove!\xe2\x80\x94share a carriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where you\xe2\x80\x99ll go wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst. \xe2\x80\x9cPutting virgins among\r\nmatrons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long should you think that an expedition like that would take,\r\nHirst?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hewet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom twelve to sixteen hours I would say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst. \xe2\x80\x9cThe time\r\nusually occupied by a first confinement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will need considerable organisation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet. He was now\r\npadding softly round the room, and stopped to stir the books on the\r\ntable. They lay heaped one upon another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall want some poets too,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cNot Gibbon; no; d\xe2\x80\x99you\r\nhappen to have _Modern Love_ or _John Donne_? You see, I contemplate\r\npauses when people get tired of looking at the view, and then it would\r\nbe nice to read something rather difficult aloud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Paley _will_ enjoy herself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Paley will enjoy it certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s one of the\r\nsaddest things I know\xe2\x80\x94the way elderly ladies cease to read poetry. And\r\nyet how appropriate this is:\r\n\r\nI speak as one who plumbs\r\n    Life\xe2\x80\x99s dim profound,\r\nOne who at length can sound\r\n    Clear views and certain.\r\nBut\xe2\x80\x94after love what comes?\r\n    A scene that lours,\r\nA few sad vacant hours,\r\n    And then, the Curtain.\r\n\r\n\r\nI daresay Mrs. Paley is the only one of us who can really understand\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll ask her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Hewet, if you must go to bed,\r\ndraw my curtain. Few things distress me more than the moonlight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHewet retreated, pressing the poems of Thomas Hardy beneath his arm,\r\nand in their beds next door to each other both the young men were soon\r\nasleep.\r\n\r\nBetween the extinction of Hewet\xe2\x80\x99s candle and the rising of a dusky\r\nSpanish boy who was the first to survey the desolation of the hotel in\r\nthe early morning, a few hours of silence intervened. One could almost\r\nhear a hundred people breathing deeply, and however wakeful and\r\nrestless it would have been hard to escape sleep in the middle of so\r\nmuch sleep. Looking out of the windows, there was only darkness to be\r\nseen. All over the shadowed half of the world people lay prone, and a\r\nfew flickering lights in empty streets marked the places where their\r\ncities were built. Red and yellow omnibuses were crowding each other in\r\nPiccadilly; sumptuous women were rocking at a standstill; but here in\r\nthe darkness an owl flitted from tree to tree, and when the breeze\r\nlifted the branches the moon flashed as if it were a torch. Until all\r\npeople should awake again the houseless animals were abroad, the tigers\r\nand the stags, and the elephants coming down in the darkness to drink\r\nat pools. The wind at night blowing over the hills and woods was purer\r\nand fresher than the wind by day, and the earth, robbed of detail, more\r\nmysterious than the earth coloured and divided by roads and fields. For\r\nsix hours this profound beauty existed, and then as the east grew\r\nwhiter and whiter the ground swam to the surface, the roads were\r\nrevealed, the smoke rose and the people stirred, and the sun shone upon\r\nthe windows of the hotel at Santa Marina until they were uncurtained,\r\nand the gong blaring all through the house gave notice of breakfast.\r\n\r\nDirectly breakfast was over, the ladies as usual circled vaguely,\r\npicking up papers and putting them down again, about the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what are you going to do to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Elliot drifting up\r\nagainst Miss Warrington.\r\n\r\nMrs. Elliot, the wife of Hughling the Oxford Don, was a short woman,\r\nwhose expression was habitually plaintive. Her eyes moved from thing to\r\nthing as though they never found anything sufficiently pleasant to rest\r\nupon for any length of time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to try to get Aunt Emma out into the town,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s not seen a thing yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI call it so spirited of her at her age,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot, \xe2\x80\x9ccoming\r\nall this way from her own fireside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we always tell her she\xe2\x80\x99ll die on board ship,\xe2\x80\x9d Susan replied. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\nwas born on one,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the old days,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot, \xe2\x80\x9ca great many people were. I\r\nalways pity the poor women so! We\xe2\x80\x99ve got a lot to complain of!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nshook her head. Her eyes wandered about the table, and she remarked\r\nirrelevantly, \xe2\x80\x9cThe poor little Queen of Holland! Newspaper reporters\r\npractically, one may say, at her bedroom door!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you talking of the Queen of Holland?\xe2\x80\x9d said the pleasant voice of\r\nMiss Allan, who was searching for the thick pages of _The Times_ among\r\na litter of thin foreign sheets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always envy any one who lives in such an excessively flat country,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow very strange!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cI find a flat country so\r\ndepressing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you can\xe2\x80\x99t be very happy here then, Miss Allan,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Allan, \xe2\x80\x9cI am exceedingly fond of\r\nmountains.\xe2\x80\x9d Perceiving _The Times_ at some distance, she moved off to\r\nsecure it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must find my husband,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot, fidgeting away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I must go to my aunt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Warrington, and taking up the\r\nduties of the day they moved away.\r\n\r\nWhether the flimsiness of foreign sheets and the coarseness of their\r\ntype is any proof of frivolity and ignorance, there is no doubt that\r\nEnglish people scarce consider news read there as news, any more than a\r\nprogramme bought from a man in the street inspires confidence in what\r\nit says. A very respectable elderly pair, having inspected the long\r\ntables of newspapers, did not think it worth their while to read more\r\nthan the headlines.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe debate on the fifteenth should have reached us by now,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nThornbury murmured. Mr. Thornbury, who was beautifully clean and had\r\nred rubbed into his handsome worn face like traces of paint on a\r\nweather-beaten wooden figure, looked over his glasses and saw that Miss\r\nAllan had _The Times_.\r\n\r\nThe couple therefore sat themselves down in arm-chairs and waited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there\xe2\x80\x99s Mr. Hewet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Hewet,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cdo come and sit by us. I was telling my husband how much\r\nyou reminded me of a dear old friend of mine\xe2\x80\x94Mary Umpleby. She was a\r\nmost delightful woman, I assure you. She grew roses. We used to stay\r\nwith her in the old days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo young man likes to have it said that he resembles an elderly\r\nspinster,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Thornbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Hewet, \xe2\x80\x9cI always think it a compliment to\r\nremind people of some one else. But Miss Umpleby\xe2\x80\x94why did she grow\r\nroses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, poor thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s a long story. She had\r\ngone through dreadful sorrows. At one time I think she would have lost\r\nher senses if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for her garden. The soil was very much\r\nagainst her\xe2\x80\x94a blessing in disguise; she had to be up at dawn\xe2\x80\x94out in all\r\nweathers. And then there are creatures that eat roses. But she\r\ntriumphed. She always did. She was a brave soul.\xe2\x80\x9d She sighed deeply but\r\nat the same time with resignation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not realise that I was monopolising the paper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Allan,\r\ncoming up to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were so anxious to read about the debate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury,\r\naccepting it on behalf of her husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne doesn\xe2\x80\x99t realise how interesting a debate can be until one has sons\r\nin the navy. My interests are equally balanced, though; I have sons in\r\nthe army too; and one son who makes speeches at the Union\xe2\x80\x94my baby!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHirst would know him, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Hirst has such an interesting face,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nfeel one ought to be very clever to talk to him. Well, William?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nenquired, for Mr. Thornbury grunted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re making a mess of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Thornbury. He had reached the\r\nsecond column of the report, a spasmodic column, for the Irish members\r\nhad been brawling three weeks ago at Westminster over a question of\r\nnaval efficiency. After a disturbed paragraph or two, the column of\r\nprint once more ran smoothly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have read it?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Thornbury asked Miss Allan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am ashamed to say I have only read about the discoveries in\r\nCrete,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Allan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but I would give so much to realise the ancient world!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs.\r\nThornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cNow that we old people are alone,\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re on our second\r\nhoneymoon,\xe2\x80\x94I am really going to put myself to school again. After all\r\nwe are _founded_ on the past, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we, Mr. Hewet? My soldier son says\r\nthat there is still a great deal to be learnt from Hannibal. One ought\r\nto know so much more than one does. Somehow when I read the paper, I\r\nbegin with the debates first, and, before I\xe2\x80\x99ve done, the door always\r\nopens\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re a very large party at home\xe2\x80\x94and so one never does think\r\nenough about the ancients and all they\xe2\x80\x99ve done for us. But _you_ begin\r\nat the beginning, Miss Allan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I think of the Greeks I think of them as naked black men,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMiss Allan, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich is quite incorrect, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you, Mr. Hirst?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury, perceiving that the gaunt\r\nyoung man was near. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure you read everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confine myself to cricket and crime,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst. \xe2\x80\x9cThe worst of\r\ncoming from the upper classes,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cis that one\xe2\x80\x99s friends\r\nare never killed in railway accidents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Thornbury threw down the paper, and emphatically dropped his\r\neyeglasses. The sheets fell in the middle of the group, and were eyed\r\nby them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not gone well?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his wife solicitously.\r\n\r\nHewet picked up one sheet and read, \xe2\x80\x9cA lady was walking yesterday in\r\nthe streets of Westminster when she perceived a cat in the window of a\r\ndeserted house. The famished animal\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be out of it anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Thornbury interrupted peevishly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCats are often forgotten,\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Allan remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember, William, the Prime Minister has reserved his answer,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMrs. Thornbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the age of eighty, Mr. Joshua Harris of Eeles Park, Brondesbury,\r\nhas had a son,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c. . . The famished animal, which had been noticed by workmen for some\r\ndays, was rescued, but\xe2\x80\x94by Jove! it bit the man\xe2\x80\x99s hand to pieces!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWild with hunger, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Miss Allan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re all neglecting the chief advantage of being abroad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nHughling Elliot, who had joined the group. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might read your news in\r\nFrench, which is equivalent to reading no news at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Elliot had a profound knowledge of Coptic, which he concealed as\r\nfar as possible, and quoted French phrases so exquisitely that it was\r\nhard to believe that he could also speak the ordinary tongue. He had an\r\nimmense respect for the French.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComing?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the two young men. \xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to start before it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreally hot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg of you not to walk in the heat, Hugh,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife pleaded, giving\r\nhim an angular parcel enclosing half a chicken and some raisins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHewet will be our barometer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will melt before I\r\nshall.\xe2\x80\x9d Indeed, if so much as a drop had melted off his spare ribs, the\r\nbones would have lain bare. The ladies were left alone now, surrounding\r\n_The Times_ which lay upon the floor. Miss Allan looked at her father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwatch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen minutes to eleven,\xe2\x80\x9d she observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWork?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Thornbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWork,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Miss Allan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a fine creature she is!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Thornbury, as the square\r\nfigure in its manly coat withdrew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99m sure she has a hard life,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Elliot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it _is_ a hard life,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cUnmarried\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x94earning their livings\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s the hardest life of all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet she seems pretty cheerful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cI envy her her\r\nknowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that isn\xe2\x80\x99t what women want,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid it\xe2\x80\x99s all a great many can hope to have,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs.\r\nThornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe that there are more of us than ever now. Sir\r\nHarley Lethbridge was telling me only the other day how difficult it is\r\nto find boys for the navy\xe2\x80\x94partly because of their teeth, it is true.\r\nAnd I have heard young women talk quite openly of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDreadful, dreadful!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cThe crown, as one may\r\ncall it, of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s life. I, who know what it is to be childless\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe sighed and ceased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we must not be hard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cThe conditions are so\r\nmuch changed since I was a young woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely _maternity_ does not change,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn some ways we can learn a great deal from the young,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nThornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cI learn so much from my own daughters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that Hughling really doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nthen he has his work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen without children can do so much for the children of others,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nobserved Mrs. Thornbury gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sketch a great deal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that isn\xe2\x80\x99t really an\r\noccupation. It\xe2\x80\x99s so disconcerting to find girls just beginning doing\r\nbetter than one does oneself! And nature\xe2\x80\x99s difficult\xe2\x80\x94very difficult!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there not institutions\xe2\x80\x94clubs\xe2\x80\x94that you could help?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs.\r\nThornbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are so exhausting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cI look strong, because of\r\nmy colour; but I\xe2\x80\x99m not; the youngest of eleven never is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the mother is careful before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury judicially,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthere is no reason why the size of the family should make any\r\ndifference. And there is no training like the training that brothers\r\nand sisters give each other. I am sure of that. I have seen it with my\r\nown children. My eldest boy Ralph, for instance\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Mrs. Elliot was inattentive to the elder lady\xe2\x80\x99s experience, and her\r\neyes wandered about the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother had two miscarriages, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cThe first\r\nbecause she met one of those great dancing bears\xe2\x80\x94they shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nallowed; the other\xe2\x80\x94it was a horrid story\xe2\x80\x94our cook had a child and there\r\nwas a dinner party. So I put my dyspepsia down to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a miscarriage is so much worse than a confinement,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Thornbury\r\nmurmured absentmindedly, adjusting her spectacles and picking up _The\r\nTimes_. Mrs. Elliot rose and fluttered away.\r\n\r\nWhen she had heard what one of the million voices speaking in the paper\r\nhad to say, and noticed that a cousin of hers had married a clergyman\r\nat Minehead\xe2\x80\x94ignoring the drunken women, the golden animals of Crete,\r\nthe movements of battalions, the dinners, the reforms, the fires, the\r\nindignant, the learned and benevolent, Mrs. Thornbury went upstairs to\r\nwrite a letter for the mail.\r\n\r\nThe paper lay directly beneath the clock, the two together seeming to\r\nrepresent stability in a changing world. Mr. Perrott passed through;\r\nMr. Venning poised for a second on the edge of a table. Mrs. Paley was\r\nwheeled past. Susan followed. Mr. Venning strolled after her.\r\nPortuguese military families, their clothes suggesting late rising in\r\nuntidy bedrooms, trailed across, attended by confidential nurses\r\ncarrying noisy children. As midday drew on, and the sun beat straight\r\nupon the roof, an eddy of great flies droned in a circle; iced drinks\r\nwere served under the palms; the long blinds were pulled down with a\r\nshriek, turning all the light yellow. The clock now had a silent hall\r\nto tick in, and an audience of four or five somnolent merchants. By\r\ndegrees white figures with shady hats came in at the door, admitting a\r\nwedge of the hot summer day, and shutting it out again. After resting\r\nin the dimness for a minute, they went upstairs. Simultaneously, the\r\nclock wheezed one, and the gong sounded, beginning softly, working\r\nitself into a frenzy, and ceasing. There was a pause. Then all those\r\nwho had gone upstairs came down; cripples came, planting both feet on\r\nthe same step lest they should slip; prim little girls came, holding\r\nthe nurse\xe2\x80\x99s finger; fat old men came still buttoning waistcoats. The\r\ngong had been sounded in the garden, and by degrees recumbent figures\r\nrose and strolled in to eat, since the time had come for them to feed\r\nagain. There were pools and bars of shade in the garden even at midday,\r\nwhere two or three visitors could lie working or talking at their ease.\r\n\r\nOwing to the heat of the day, luncheon was generally a silent meal,\r\nwhen people observed their neighbors and took stock of any new faces\r\nthere might be, hazarding guesses as to who they were and what they\r\ndid. Mrs. Paley, although well over seventy and crippled in the legs,\r\nenjoyed her food and the peculiarities of her fellow-beings. She was\r\nseated at a small table with Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to say what _she_ is!\xe2\x80\x9d she chuckled, surveying a tall\r\nwoman dressed conspicuously in white, with paint in the hollows of her\r\ncheeks, who was always late, and always attended by a shabby female\r\nfollower, at which remark Susan blushed, and wondered why her aunt said\r\nsuch things.\r\n\r\nLunch went on methodically, until each of the seven courses was left in\r\nfragments and the fruit was merely a toy, to be peeled and sliced as a\r\nchild destroys a daisy, petal by petal. The food served as an\r\nextinguisher upon any faint flame of the human spirit that might\r\nsurvive the midday heat, but Susan sat in her room afterwards, turning\r\nover and over the delightful fact that Mr. Venning had come to her in\r\nthe garden, and had sat there quite half an hour while she read aloud\r\nto her aunt. Men and women sought different corners where they could\r\nlie unobserved, and from two to four it might be said without\r\nexaggeration that the hotel was inhabited by bodies without souls.\r\nDisastrous would have been the result if a fire or a death had suddenly\r\ndemanded something heroic of human nature, but tragedies come in the\r\nhungry hours. Towards four o\xe2\x80\x99clock the human spirit again began to lick\r\nthe body, as a flame licks a black promontory of coal. Mrs. Paley felt\r\nit unseemly to open her toothless jaw so widely, though there was no\r\none near, and Mrs. Elliot surveyed her round flushed face anxiously in\r\nthe looking-glass.\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later, having removed the traces of sleep, they met each\r\nother in the hall, and Mrs. Paley observed that she was going to have\r\nher tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou like your tea too, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and invited Mrs. Elliot,\r\nwhose husband was still out, to join her at a special table which she\r\nhad placed for her under a tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little silver goes a long way in this country,\xe2\x80\x9d she chuckled.\r\n\r\nShe sent Susan back to fetch another cup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have such excellent biscuits here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, contemplating a\r\nplateful. \xe2\x80\x9cNot sweet biscuits, which I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\xe2\x80\x94dry biscuits . . .\r\nHave you been sketching?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ve done two or three little daubs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot, speaking\r\nrather louder than usual. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s so difficult after Oxfordshire,\r\nwhere there are so many trees. The light\xe2\x80\x99s so strong here. Some people\r\nadmire it, I know, but I find it very fatiguing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really don\xe2\x80\x99t need cooking, Susan,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Paley, when her niece\r\nreturned. \xe2\x80\x9cI must trouble you to move me.\xe2\x80\x9d Everything had to be moved.\r\nFinally the old lady was placed so that the light wavered over her, as\r\nthough she were a fish in a net. Susan poured out tea, and was just\r\nremarking that they were having hot weather in Wiltshire too, when Mr.\r\nVenning asked whether he might join them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so nice to find a young man who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t despise tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nPaley, regaining her good humour. \xe2\x80\x9cOne of my nephews the other day\r\nasked for a glass of sherry\xe2\x80\x94at five o\xe2\x80\x99clock! I told him he could get it\r\nat the public house round the corner, but not in my drawing room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather go without lunch than tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Venning. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\nstrictly true. I want both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Venning was a dark young man, about thirty-two years of age, very\r\nslapdash and confident in his manner, although at this moment obviously\r\na little excited. His friend Mr. Perrott was a barrister, and as Mr.\r\nPerrott refused to go anywhere without Mr. Venning it was necessary,\r\nwhen Mr. Perrott came to Santa Marina about a Company, for Mr. Venning\r\nto come too. He was a barrister also, but he loathed a profession which\r\nkept him indoors over books, and directly his widowed mother died he\r\nwas going, so he confided to Susan, to take up flying seriously, and\r\nbecome partner in a large business for making aeroplanes. The talk\r\nrambled on. It dealt, of course, with the beauties and singularities of\r\nthe place, the streets, the people, and the quantities of unowned\r\nyellow dogs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think it dreadfully cruel the way they treat dogs in this\r\ncountry?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Paley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d have \xe2\x80\x99em all shot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Venning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but the darling puppies,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJolly little chaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Venning. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got nothing\r\nto eat.\xe2\x80\x9d A great wedge of cake was handed Susan on the point of a\r\ntrembling knife. Her hand trembled too as she took it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have such a dear dog at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy parrot can\xe2\x80\x99t stand dogs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Paley, with the air of one\r\nmaking a confidence. \xe2\x80\x9cI always suspect that he (or she) was teased by a\r\ndog when I was abroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t get far this morning, Miss Warrington,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Venning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was hot,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. Their conversation became private, owing to\r\nMrs. Paley\xe2\x80\x99s deafness and the long sad history which Mrs. Elliot had\r\nembarked upon of a wire-haired terrier, white with just one black spot,\r\nbelonging to an uncle of hers, which had committed suicide. \xe2\x80\x9cAnimals do\r\ncommit suicide,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed, as if she asserted a painful fact.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we explore the town this evening?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Venning suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy aunt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Susan began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou deserve a holiday,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re always doing things for other\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that\xe2\x80\x99s my life,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, under cover of refilling the teapot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no one\xe2\x80\x99s life,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned, \xe2\x80\x9cno young person\xe2\x80\x99s. You\xe2\x80\x99ll come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to come,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\nAt this moment Mrs. Elliot looked up and exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Hugh! He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbringing some one,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would like some tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Paley. \xe2\x80\x9cSusan, run and get some\r\ncups\xe2\x80\x94there are the two young men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re thirsting for tea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know Mr. Ambrose,\r\nHilda? We met on the hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe dragged me in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley, \xe2\x80\x9cor I should have been ashamed. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ndusty and dirty and disagreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed to his boots which were\r\nwhite with dust, while a dejected flower drooping in his buttonhole,\r\nlike an exhausted animal over a gate, added to the effect of length and\r\nuntidiness. He was introduced to the others. Mr. Hewet and Mr. Hirst\r\nbrought chairs, and tea began again, Susan pouring cascades of water\r\nfrom pot to pot, always cheerfully, and with the competence of long\r\nuse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy wife\xe2\x80\x99s brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Ridley explained to Hilda, whom he failed to\r\nremember, \xe2\x80\x9chas a house here, which he has lent us. I was sitting on a\r\nrock thinking of nothing at all when Elliot started up like a fairy in\r\na pantomime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur chicken got into the salt,\xe2\x80\x9d Hewet said dolefully to Susan. \xe2\x80\x9cNor is\r\nit true that bananas include moisture as well as sustenance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHirst was already drinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve been cursing you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley in answer to Mrs. Elliot\xe2\x80\x99s kind\r\nenquiries about his wife. \xe2\x80\x9cYou tourists eat up all the eggs, Helen\r\ntells me. That\xe2\x80\x99s an eye-sore too\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he nodded his head at the hotel.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDisgusting luxury, I call it. We live with pigs in the drawing-room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe food is not at all what it ought to be, considering the price,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mrs. Paley seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cBut unless one goes to a hotel where is one\r\nto go to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ridley. \xe2\x80\x9cI often wish I had! Everyone ought to\r\nstay at home. But, of course, they won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Paley conceived a certain grudge against Ridley, who seemed to be\r\ncriticising her habits after an acquaintance of five minutes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe in foreign travel myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she stated, \xe2\x80\x9cif one knows one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnative land, which I think I can honestly say I do. I should not allow\r\nany one to travel until they had visited Kent and Dorsetshire\xe2\x80\x94Kent for\r\nthe hops, and Dorsetshire for its old stone cottages. There is nothing\r\nto compare with them here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I always think that some people like the flat and other people\r\nlike the downs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot rather vaguely.\r\n\r\nHirst, who had been eating and drinking without interruption, now lit a\r\ncigarette, and observed, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, but we\xe2\x80\x99re all agreed by this time that\r\nnature\xe2\x80\x99s a mistake. She\xe2\x80\x99s either very ugly, appallingly uncomfortable,\r\nor absolutely terrifying. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know which alarms me most\xe2\x80\x94a cow or a\r\ntree. I once met a cow in a field by night. The creature looked at me.\r\nI assure you it turned my hair grey. It\xe2\x80\x99s a disgrace that the animals\r\nshould be allowed to go at large.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did the cow think of _him_?\xe2\x80\x9d Venning mumbled to Susan, who\r\nimmediately decided in her own mind that Mr. Hirst was a dreadful young\r\nman, and that although he had such an air of being clever he probably\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t as clever as Arthur, in the ways that really matter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t it Wilde who discovered the fact that nature makes no allowance\r\nfor hip-bones?\xe2\x80\x9d enquired Hughling Elliot. He knew by this time exactly\r\nwhat scholarships and distinction Hirst enjoyed, and had formed a very\r\nhigh opinion of his capacities.\r\n\r\nBut Hirst merely drew his lips together very tightly and made no reply.\r\n\r\nRidley conjectured that it was now permissible for him to take his\r\nleave. Politeness required him to thank Mrs. Elliot for his tea, and to\r\nadd, with a wave of his hand, \xe2\x80\x9cYou must come up and see us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wave included both Hirst and Hewet, and Hewet answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nlike it immensely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe party broke up, and Susan, who had never felt so happy in her life,\r\nwas just about to start for her walk in the town with Arthur, when Mrs.\r\nPaley beckoned her back. She could not understand from the book how\r\nDouble Demon patience is played; and suggested that if they sat down\r\nand worked it out together it would fill up the time nicely before\r\ndinner.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nAmong the promises which Mrs. Ambrose had made her niece should she\r\nstay was a room cut off from the rest of the house, large, private\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nroom in which she could play, read, think, defy the world, a fortress\r\nas well as a sanctuary. Rooms, she knew, became more like worlds than\r\nrooms at the age of twenty-four. Her judgment was correct, and when she\r\nshut the door Rachel entered an enchanted place, where the poets sang\r\nand things fell into their right proportions. Some days after the\r\nvision of the hotel by night she was sitting alone, sunk in an\r\narm-chair, reading a brightly-covered red volume lettered on the back\r\n_Works of Henrik Ibsen_. Music was open on the piano, and books of\r\nmusic rose in two jagged pillars on the floor; but for the moment music\r\nwas deserted.\r\n\r\nFar from looking bored or absent-minded, her eyes were concentrated\r\nalmost sternly upon the page, and from her breathing, which was slow\r\nbut repressed, it could be seen that her whole body was constrained by\r\nthe working of her mind. At last she shut the book sharply, lay back,\r\nand drew a deep breath, expressive of the wonder which always marks the\r\ntransition from the imaginary world to the real world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I want to know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cis this: What is the truth?\r\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the truth of it all?\xe2\x80\x9d She was speaking partly as herself, and\r\npartly as the heroine of the play she had just read. The landscape\r\noutside, because she had seen nothing but print for the space of two\r\nhours, now appeared amazingly solid and clear, but although there were\r\nmen on the hill washing the trunks of olive trees with a white liquid,\r\nfor the moment she herself was the most vivid thing in it\xe2\x80\x94an heroic\r\nstatue in the middle of the foreground, dominating the view. Ibsen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nplays always left her in that condition. She acted them for days at a\r\ntime, greatly to Helen\xe2\x80\x99s amusement; and then it would be Meredith\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nturn and she became Diana of the Crossways. But Helen was aware that it\r\nwas not all acting, and that some sort of change was taking place in\r\nthe human being. When Rachel became tired of the rigidity of her pose\r\non the back of the chair, she turned round, slid comfortably down into\r\nit, and gazed out over the furniture through the window opposite which\r\nopened on the garden. (Her mind wandered away from Nora, but she went\r\non thinking of things that the book suggested to her, of women and\r\nlife.)\r\n\r\nDuring the three months she had been here she had made up considerably,\r\nas Helen meant she should, for time spent in interminable walks round\r\nsheltered gardens, and the household gossip of her aunts. But Mrs.\r\nAmbrose would have been the first to disclaim any influence, or indeed\r\nany belief that to influence was within her power. She saw her less\r\nshy, and less serious, which was all to the good, and the violent leaps\r\nand the interminable mazes which had led to that result were usually\r\nnot even guessed at by her. Talk was the medicine she trusted to, talk\r\nabout everything, talk that was free, unguarded, and as candid as a\r\nhabit of talking with men made natural in her own case. Nor did she\r\nencourage those habits of unselfishness and amiability founded upon\r\ninsincerity which are put at so high a value in mixed households of men\r\nand women. She desired that Rachel should think, and for this reason\r\noffered books and discouraged too entire a dependence upon Bach and\r\nBeethoven and Wagner. But when Mrs. Ambrose would have suggested Defoe,\r\nMaupassant, or some spacious chronicle of family life, Rachel chose\r\nmodern books, books in shiny yellow covers, books with a great deal of\r\ngilding on the back, which were tokens in her aunt\xe2\x80\x99s eyes of harsh\r\nwrangling and disputes about facts which had no such importance as the\r\nmoderns claimed for them. But she did not interfere. Rachel read what\r\nshe chose, reading with the curious literalness of one to whom written\r\nsentences are unfamiliar, and handling words as though they were made\r\nof wood, separately of great importance, and possessed of shapes like\r\ntables or chairs. In this way she came to conclusions, which had to be\r\nremodelled according to the adventures of the day, and were indeed\r\nrecast as liberally as any one could desire, leaving always a small\r\ngrain of belief behind them.\r\n\r\nIbsen was succeeded by a novel such as Mrs. Ambrose detested, whose\r\npurpose was to distribute the guilt of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s downfall upon the\r\nright shoulders; a purpose which was achieved, if the reader\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndiscomfort were any proof of it. She threw the book down, looked out of\r\nthe window, turned away from the window, and relapsed into an\r\narm-chair.\r\n\r\nThe morning was hot, and the exercise of reading left her mind\r\ncontracting and expanding like the main-spring of a clock, and the\r\nsmall noises of midday, which one can ascribe to no definite cause, in\r\na regular rhythm. It was all very real, very big, very impersonal, and\r\nafter a moment or two she began to raise her first finger and to let it\r\nfall on the arm of her chair so as to bring back to herself some\r\nconsciousness of her own existence. She was next overcome by the\r\nunspeakable queerness of the fact that she should be sitting in an\r\narm-chair, in the morning, in the middle of the world. Who were the\r\npeople moving in the house\xe2\x80\x94moving things from one place to another? And\r\nlife, what was that? It was only a light passing over the surface and\r\nvanishing, as in time she would vanish, though the furniture in the\r\nroom would remain. Her dissolution became so complete that she could\r\nnot raise her finger any more, and sat perfectly still, listening and\r\nlooking always at the same spot. It became stranger and stranger. She\r\nwas overcome with awe that things should exist at all. . . . She forgot\r\nthat she had any fingers to raise. . . . The things that existed were\r\nso immense and so desolate. . . . She continued to be conscious of\r\nthese vast masses of substance for a long stretch of time, the clock\r\nstill ticking in the midst of the universal silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said mechanically, for a string in her brain seemed to\r\nbe pulled by a persistent knocking at the door. With great slowness the\r\ndoor opened and a tall human being came towards her, holding out her\r\narm and saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to say to this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe utter absurdity of a woman coming into a room with a piece of paper\r\nin her hand amazed Rachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to answer, or who Terence Hewet is,\xe2\x80\x9d Helen\r\ncontinued, in the toneless voice of a ghost. She put a paper before\r\nRachel on which were written the incredible words:\r\n\r\nDEAR MRS. AMBROSE\xe2\x80\x94I am getting up a picnic for next Friday, when we\r\npropose to start at eleven-thirty if the weather is fine, and to make\r\nthe ascent of Monte Rosa. It will take some time, but the view should\r\nbe magnificent. It would give me great pleasure if you and Miss Vinrace\r\nwould consent to be of the party.\xe2\x80\x94Yours sincerely,\r\n\r\n\r\nTERENCE HEWET\r\n\r\n\r\nRachel read the words aloud to make herself believe in them. For the\r\nsame reason she put her hand on Helen\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBooks\xe2\x80\x94books\xe2\x80\x94books,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, in her absent-minded way. \xe2\x80\x9cMore new\r\nbooks\xe2\x80\x94I wonder what you find in them. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the second time Rachel read the letter, but to herself. This time,\r\ninstead of seeming vague as ghosts, each word was astonishingly\r\nprominent; they came out as the tops of mountains come through a mist.\r\n_Friday_\xe2\x80\x94_eleven-thirty_\xe2\x80\x94_Miss Vinrace_. The blood began to run in her\r\nveins; she felt her eyes brighten.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must go,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, rather surprising Helen by her decision. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nmust certainly go\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94such was the relief of finding that things still\r\nhappened, and indeed they appeared the brighter for the mist\r\nsurrounding them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonte Rosa\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the mountain over there, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen; \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nHewet\xe2\x80\x94who\xe2\x80\x99s he? One of the young men Ridley met, I suppose. Shall I say\r\nyes, then? It may be dreadfully dull.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took the letter back and went, for the messenger was waiting for\r\nher answer.\r\n\r\nThe party which had been suggested a few nights ago in Mr. Hirst\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedroom had taken shape and was the source of great satisfaction to Mr.\r\nHewet, who had seldom used his practical abilities, and was pleased to\r\nfind them equal to the strain. His invitations had been universally\r\naccepted, which was the more encouraging as they had been issued\r\nagainst Hirst\xe2\x80\x99s advice to people who were very dull, not at all suited\r\nto each other, and sure not to come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he twirled and untwirled a note signed Helen\r\nAmbrose, \xe2\x80\x9cthe gifts needed to make a great commander have been absurdly\r\noverrated. About half the intellectual effort which is needed to review\r\na book of modern poetry has enabled me to get together seven or eight\r\npeople, of opposite sexes, at the same spot at the same hour on the\r\nsame day. What else is generalship, Hirst? What more did Wellington do\r\non the field of Waterloo? It\xe2\x80\x99s like counting the number of pebbles of a\r\npath, tedious but not difficult.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was sitting in his bedroom, one leg over the arm of the chair, and\r\nHirst was writing a letter opposite. Hirst was quick to point out that\r\nall the difficulties remained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor instance, here are two women you\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen. Suppose one of\r\nthem suffers from mountain-sickness, as my sister does, and the other\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the women are for you,\xe2\x80\x9d Hewet interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI asked them solely\r\nfor your benefit. What you want, Hirst, you know, is the society of\r\nyoung women of your own age. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to get on with women,\r\nwhich is a great defect, considering that half the world consists of\r\nwomen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHirst groaned that he was quite aware of that.\r\n\r\nBut Hewet\xe2\x80\x99s complacency was a little chilled as he walked with Hirst to\r\nthe place where a general meeting had been appointed. He wondered why\r\non earth he had asked these people, and what one really expected to get\r\nfrom bunching human beings up together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCows,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cdraw together in a field; ships in a calm; and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re just the same when we\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing else to do. But why do we do\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x94is it to prevent ourselves from seeing to the bottom of things\xe2\x80\x9d (he\r\nstopped by a stream and began stirring it with his walking-stick and\r\nclouding the water with mud), \xe2\x80\x9cmaking cities and mountains and whole\r\nuniverses out of nothing, or do we really love each other, or do we, on\r\nthe other hand, live in a state of perpetual uncertainty, knowing\r\nnothing, leaping from moment to moment as from world to world?\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nis, on the whole, the view _I_ incline to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe jumped over the stream; Hirst went round and joined him, remarking\r\nthat he had long ceased to look for the reason of any human action.\r\n\r\nHalf a mile further, they came to a group of plane trees and the\r\nsalmon-pink farmhouse standing by the stream which had been chosen as\r\nmeeting-place. It was a shady spot, lying conveniently just where the\r\nhill sprung out from the flat. Between the thin stems of the plane\r\ntrees the young men could see little knots of donkeys pasturing, and a\r\ntall woman rubbing the nose of one of them, while another woman was\r\nkneeling by the stream lapping water out of her palms.\r\n\r\nAs they entered the shady place, Helen looked up and then held out her\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must introduce myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am Mrs. Ambrose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving shaken hands, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my niece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRachel approached awkwardly. She held out her hand, but withdrew it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all wet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nScarcely had they spoken, when the first carriage drew up.\r\n\r\nThe donkeys were quickly jerked into attention, and the second carriage\r\narrived. By degrees the grove filled with people\xe2\x80\x94the Elliots, the\r\nThornburys, Mr. Venning and Susan, Miss Allan, Evelyn Murgatroyd, and\r\nMr. Perrott. Mr. Hirst acted the part of hoarse energetic sheep-dog. By\r\nmeans of a few words of caustic Latin he had the animals marshalled,\r\nand by inclining a sharp shoulder he lifted the ladies. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat Hewet\r\nfails to understand,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cis that we must break the back of\r\nthe ascent before midday.\xe2\x80\x9d He was assisting a young lady, by name\r\nEvelyn Murgatroyd, as he spoke. She rose light as a bubble to her seat.\r\nWith a feather drooping from a broad-brimmed hat, in white from top to\r\ntoe, she looked like a gallant lady of the time of Charles the First\r\nleading royalist troops into action.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRide with me,\xe2\x80\x9d she commanded; and, as soon as Hirst had swung himself\r\nacross a mule, the two started, leading the cavalcade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not to call me Miss Murgatroyd. I hate it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\nname\xe2\x80\x99s Evelyn. What\xe2\x80\x99s yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSt. John,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Evelyn. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s your friend\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis initials being R. S. T., we call him Monk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re all too clever,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhich way? Pick me a branch.\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s canter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gave her donkey a sharp cut with a switch and started forward. The\r\nfull and romantic career of Evelyn Murgatroyd is best hit off by her\r\nown words, \xe2\x80\x9cCall me Evelyn and I\xe2\x80\x99ll call you St. John.\xe2\x80\x9d She said that\r\non very slight provocation\xe2\x80\x94her surname was enough\xe2\x80\x94but although a great\r\nmany young men had answered her already with considerable spirit she\r\nwent on saying it and making choice of none. But her donkey stumbled to\r\na jog-trot, and she had to ride in advance alone, for the path when it\r\nbegan to ascend one of the spines of the hill became narrow and\r\nscattered with stones. The cavalcade wound on like a jointed\r\ncaterpillar, tufted with the white parasols of the ladies, and the\r\npanama hats of the gentlemen. At one point where the ground rose\r\nsharply, Evelyn M. jumped off, threw her reins to the native boy, and\r\nadjured St. John Hirst to dismount too. Their example was followed by\r\nthose who felt the need of stretching.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see any need to get off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Allan to Mrs. Elliot just\r\nbehind her, \xe2\x80\x9cconsidering the difficulty I had getting on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese little donkeys stand anything, _n\xe2\x80\x99est-ce pas_?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Elliot\r\naddressed the guide, who obligingly bowed his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFlowers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen, stooping to pick the lovely little bright\r\nflowers which grew separately here and there. \xe2\x80\x9cYou pinch their leaves\r\nand then they smell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, laying one on Miss Allan\xe2\x80\x99s knee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t we met before?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Allan, looking at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was taking it for granted,\xe2\x80\x9d Helen laughed, for in the confusion of\r\nmeeting they had not been introduced.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow sensible!\xe2\x80\x9d chirped Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what one would always\r\nlike\xe2\x80\x94only unfortunately it\xe2\x80\x99s not possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot possible?\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen. \xe2\x80\x9cEverything\xe2\x80\x99s possible. Who knows what\r\nmayn\xe2\x80\x99t happen before night-fall?\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, mocking the poor\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s timidity, who depended so implicitly upon one thing following\r\nanother that the mere glimpse of a world where dinner could be\r\ndisregarded, or the table moved one inch from its accustomed place,\r\nfilled her with fears for her own stability.\r\n\r\nHigher and higher they went, becoming separated from the world. The\r\nworld, when they turned to look back, flattened itself out, and was\r\nmarked with squares of thin green and grey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTowns are very small,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel remarked, obscuring the whole of Santa\r\nMarina and its suburbs with one hand. The sea filled in all the angles\r\nof the coast smoothly, breaking in a white frill, and here and there\r\nships were set firmly in the blue. The sea was stained with purple and\r\ngreen blots, and there was a glittering line upon the rim where it met\r\nthe sky. The air was very clear and silent save for the sharp noise of\r\ngrasshoppers and the hum of bees, which sounded loud in the ear as they\r\nshot past and vanished. The party halted and sat for a time in a quarry\r\non the hillside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmazingly clear,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed St. John, identifying one cleft in the\r\nland after another.\r\n\r\nEvelyn M. sat beside him, propping her chin on her hand. She surveyed\r\nthe view with a certain look of triumph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you think Garibaldi was ever up here?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked Mr. Hirst. Oh, if\r\nshe had been his bride! If, instead of a picnic party, this was a party\r\nof patriots, and she, red-shirted like the rest, had lain among grim\r\nmen, flat on the turf, aiming her gun at the white turrets beneath\r\nthem, screening her eyes to pierce through the smoke! So thinking, her\r\nfoot stirred restlessly, and she exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t call this _life_, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call life?\xe2\x80\x9d said St. John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFighting\xe2\x80\x94revolution,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, still gazing at the doomed city. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nonly care for books, I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re quite wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said St. John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain,\xe2\x80\x9d she urged, for there were no guns to be aimed at bodies, and\r\nshe turned to another kind of warfare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I care for? People,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I _am_ surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou look so awfully serious.\r\nDo let\xe2\x80\x99s be friends and tell each other what we\xe2\x80\x99re like. I hate being\r\ncautious, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut St. John was decidedly cautious, as she could see by the sudden\r\nconstriction of his lips, and had no intention of revealing his soul to\r\na young lady. \xe2\x80\x9cThe ass is eating my hat,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, and stretched\r\nout for it instead of answering her. Evelyn blushed very slightly and\r\nthen turned with some impetuosity upon Mr. Perrott, and when they\r\nmounted again it was Mr. Perrott who lifted her to her seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen one has laid the eggs one eats the omelette,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hughling\r\nElliot, exquisitely in French, a hint to the rest of them that it was\r\ntime to ride on again.\r\n\r\nThe midday sun which Hirst had foretold was beginning to beat down\r\nhotly. The higher they got the more of the sky appeared, until the\r\nmountain was only a small tent of earth against an enormous blue\r\nbackground. The English fell silent; the natives who walked beside the\r\ndonkeys broke into queer wavering songs and tossed jokes from one to\r\nthe other. The way grew very steep, and each rider kept his eyes fixed\r\non the hobbling curved form of the rider and donkey directly in front\r\nof him. Rather more strain was being put upon their bodies than is\r\nquite legitimate in a party of pleasure, and Hewet overheard one or two\r\nslightly grumbling remarks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExpeditions in such heat are perhaps a little unwise,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Elliot\r\nmurmured to Miss Allan.\r\n\r\nBut Miss Allan returned, \xe2\x80\x9cI always like to get to the top\xe2\x80\x9d; and it was\r\ntrue, although she was a big woman, stiff in the joints, and unused to\r\ndonkey-riding, but as her holidays were few she made the most of them.\r\n\r\nThe vivacious white figure rode well in front; she had somehow\r\npossessed herself of a leafy branch and wore it round her hat like a\r\ngarland. They went on for a few minutes in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe view will be wonderful,\xe2\x80\x9d Hewet assured them, turning round in his\r\nsaddle and smiling encouragement. Rachel caught his eye and smiled too.\r\nThey struggled on for some time longer, nothing being heard but the\r\nclatter of hooves striving on the loose stones. Then they saw that\r\nEvelyn was off her ass, and that Mr. Perrott was standing in the\r\nattitude of a statesman in Parliament Square, stretching an arm of\r\nstone towards the view. A little to the left of them was a low ruined\r\nwall, the stump of an Elizabethan watch-tower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have stood it much longer,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Elliot confided to Mrs.\r\nThornbury, but the excitement of being at the top in another moment and\r\nseeing the view prevented any one from answering her. One after another\r\nthey came out on the flat space at the top and stood overcome with\r\nwonder. Before them they beheld an immense space\xe2\x80\x94grey sands running\r\ninto forest, and forest merging in mountains, and mountains washed by\r\nair, the infinite distances of South America. A river ran across the\r\nplain, as flat as the land, and appearing quite as stationary. The\r\neffect of so much space was at first rather chilling. They felt\r\nthemselves very small, and for some time no one said anything. Then\r\nEvelyn exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cSplendid!\xe2\x80\x9d She took hold of the hand that was next\r\nher; it chanced to be Miss Allan\xe2\x80\x99s hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNorth\xe2\x80\x94South\xe2\x80\x94East\xe2\x80\x94West,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Allan, jerking her head slightly\r\ntowards the points of the compass.\r\n\r\nHewet, who had gone a little in front, looked up at his guests as if to\r\njustify himself for having brought them. He observed how strangely the\r\npeople standing in a row with their figures bent slightly forward and\r\ntheir clothes plastered by the wind to the shape of their bodies\r\nresembled naked statues. On their pedestal of earth they looked\r\nunfamiliar and noble, but in another moment they had broken their rank,\r\nand he had to see to the laying out of food. Hirst came to his help,\r\nand they handed packets of chicken and bread from one to another.\r\n\r\nAs St. John gave Helen her packet she looked him full in the face and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember\xe2\x80\x94two women?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you\xe2\x80\x99re the two women!\xe2\x80\x9d Hewet exclaimed, looking from Helen to\r\nRachel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour lights tempted us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen. \xe2\x80\x9cWe watched you playing cards,\r\nbut we never knew that we were being watched.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was like a thing in a play,\xe2\x80\x9d Rachel added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Hirst couldn\xe2\x80\x99t describe you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet.\r\n\r\nIt was certainly odd to have seen Helen and to find nothing to say\r\nabout her.\r\n\r\nHughling Elliot put up his eyeglass and grasped the situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know of anything more dreadful,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pulling at the joint\r\nof a chicken\xe2\x80\x99s leg, \xe2\x80\x9cthan being seen when one isn\xe2\x80\x99t conscious of it.\r\nOne feels sure one has been caught doing something ridiculous\xe2\x80\x94looking\r\nat one\xe2\x80\x99s tongue in a hansom, for instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the others ceased to look at the view, and drawing together sat\r\ndown in a circle round the baskets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet those little looking-glasses in hansoms have a fascination of\r\ntheir own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Thornbury. \xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s features look so different when\r\none can only see a bit of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere will soon be very few hansom cabs left,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nfour-wheeled cabs\xe2\x80\x94I assure you even at Oxford it\xe2\x80\x99s almost impossible to\r\nget a four-wheeled cab.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what happens to the horses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVeal pie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s high time that horses should become extinct anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hirst.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re distressingly ugly, besides being vicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Susan, who had been brought up to understand that the horse is the\r\nnoblest of God\xe2\x80\x99s creatures, could not agree, and Venning thought Hirst\r\nan unspeakable ass, but was too polite not to continue the\r\nconversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen they see us falling out of aeroplanes they get some of their own\r\nback, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fly?\xe2\x80\x9d said old Mr. Thornbury, putting on his spectacles to look at\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope to, some day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur.\r\n\r\nHere flying was discussed at length, and Mrs. Thornbury delivered an\r\nopinion which was almost a speech to the effect that it would be quite\r\nnecessary in time of war, and in England we were terribly behind-hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a young fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d she concluded, \xe2\x80\x9cI should certainly\r\nqualify.\xe2\x80\x9d It was odd to look at the little elderly lady, in her grey\r\ncoat and skirt, with a sandwich in her hand, her eyes lighting up with\r\nzeal as she imagined herself a young man in an aeroplane. For some\r\nreason, however, the talk did not run easily after this, and all they\r\nsaid was about drink and salt and the view. Suddenly Miss Allan, who\r\nwas seated with her back to the ruined wall, put down her sandwich,\r\npicked something off her neck, and remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m covered with little\r\ncreatures.\xe2\x80\x9d It was true, and the discovery was very welcome. The ants\r\nwere pouring down a glacier of loose earth heaped between the stones of\r\nthe ruin\xe2\x80\x94large brown ants with polished bodies. She held out one on the\r\nback of her hand for Helen to look at.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose they sting?\xe2\x80\x9d said Helen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not sting, but they may infest the victuals,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nAllan, and measures were taken at once to divert the ants from their\r\ncourse. At Hewet\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion it was decided to adopt the methods of\r\nmodern warfare against an invading army. The table-cloth represented\r\nthe invaded country, and round it they built barricades of baskets, set\r\nup the wine bottles in a rampart, made fortifications of bread and dug\r\nfosses of salt. When an ant got through it was exposed to a fire of\r\nbread-crumbs, until Susan pronounced that that was cruel, and rewarded\r\nthose brave spirits with spoil in the shape of tongue. Playing this\r\ngame they lost their stiffness, and even became unusually daring, for\r\nMr. Perrott, who was very shy, said, \xe2\x80\x9cPermit me,\xe2\x80\x9d and removed an ant\r\nfrom Evelyn\xe2\x80\x99s neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be no laughing matter really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Elliot\r\nconfidentially to Mrs. Thornbury, \xe2\x80\x9cif an ant did get between the vest\r\nand the skin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe noise grew suddenly more clamorous, for it was discovered that a\r\nlong line of ants had found their way on to the table-cloth by a back\r\nentrance, and if success could be gauged by noise, Hewet had every\r\nreason to think his party a success. Nevertheless he became, for no\r\nreason at all, profoundly depressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not satisfactory; they are ignoble,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, surveying\r\nhis guests from a little distance, where he was gathering together the\r\nplates. He glanced at them all, stooping and swaying and gesticulating\r\nround the table-cloth. Amiable and modest, respectable in many ways,\r\nlovable even in their contentment and desire to be kind, how mediocre\r\nthey all were, and capable of what insipid cruelty to one another!\r\nThere was Mrs. Thornbury, sweet but trivial in her maternal egoism;\r\nMrs. Elliot, perpetually complaining of her lot; her husband a mere pea\r\nin a pod; and Susan\xe2\x80\x94she had no self, and counted neither one way nor\r\nthe other; Venning was as honest and as brutal as a schoolboy; poor old\r\nThornbury merely trod his round like a horse in a mill; and the less\r\none examined into Evelyn\xe2\x80\x99s character the better, he suspected. Yet\r\nthese were the people with money, and to them rather than to others was\r\ngiven the management of the world. Put among them some one more vital,\r\nwho cared for life or for beauty, and what an agony, what a waste would\r\nthey inflict on him if he tried to share with them and not to scourge!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s Hirst,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded, coming to the figure of his friend; with\r\nhis usual little frown of concentration upon his forehead he was\r\npeeling the skin off a banana. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he\xe2\x80\x99s as ugly as sin.\xe2\x80\x9d For the\r\nugliness of St. John Hirst, and the limitations that went with it, he\r\nmade the rest in some way responsible. It was their fault that he had\r\nto live alone. Then he came to Helen, attracted to her by the sound of\r\nher laugh. She was laughing at Miss Allan. \xe2\x80\x9cYou wear combinations in\r\nthis heat?\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a voice which was meant to be private. He liked\r\nthe look of her immensely, not so much her beauty, but her largeness\r\nand simplicity, which made her stand out from the rest like a great\r\nstone woman, and he passed on in a gentler mood. His eye fell upon\r\nRachel. She was lying back rather behind the others resting on one\r\nelbow; she might have been thinking precisely the same thoughts as\r\nHewet himself. Her eyes were fixed rather sadly but not intently upon\r\nthe row of people opposite her. Hewet crawled up to her on his knees,\r\nwith a piece of bread in his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you looking at?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nShe was a little startled, but answered directly, \xe2\x80\x9cHuman beings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\nOne after another they rose and stretched themselves, and in a few\r\nminutes divided more or less into two separate parties. One of these\r\nparties was dominated by Hughling Elliot and Mrs. Thornbury, who,\r\nhaving both read the same books and considered the same questions, were\r\nnow anxious to name the places beneath them and to hang upon them\r\nstores of information about navies and armies, political parties,\r\nnatives and mineral products\xe2\x80\x94all of which combined, they said, to prove\r\nthat South America was the country of the future.\r\n\r\nEvelyn M. listened with her bright blue eyes fixed upon the oracles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow it makes one long to be a man!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\nMr. Perrott answered, surveying the plain, that a country with a future\r\nwas a very fine thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Evelyn, turning to him and drawing her glove\r\nvehemently through her fingers, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d raise a troop and conquer some\r\ngreat territory and make it splendid. You\xe2\x80\x99d want women for that. I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlove to start life from the very beginning as it ought to be\xe2\x80\x94nothing\r\nsqualid\xe2\x80\x94but great halls and gardens and splendid men and women. But\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94you only like Law Courts!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would you really be content without pretty frocks and sweets and\r\nall the things young ladies like?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Perrott, concealing a\r\ncertain amount of pain beneath his ironical manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not a young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d Evelyn flashed; she bit her underlip. \xe2\x80\x9cJust\r\nbecause I like splendid things you laugh at me. Why are there no men\r\nlike Garibaldi now?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Perrott, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t give me a chance. You think\r\nwe ought to begin things fresh. Good. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t see precisely\xe2\x80\x94conquer\r\na territory? They\xe2\x80\x99re all conquered already, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not any territory in particular,\xe2\x80\x9d Evelyn explained. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nidea, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see? We lead such tame lives. And I feel sure you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngot splendid things in you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHewet saw the scars and hollows in Mr. Perrott\xe2\x80\x99s sagacious face relax\r\npathetically. He could imagine the calculations which even then went on\r\nwithin his mind, as to whether he would be justified in asking a woman\r\nto marry him, considering that he made no more than five hundred a year\r\nat the Bar, owned no private means, and had an invalid sister to\r\nsupport. Mr. Perrott again knew that he was not \xe2\x80\x9cquite,\xe2\x80\x9d as Susan\r\nstated in her diary; not quite a gentleman she meant, for he was the\r\nson of a grocer in Leeds, had started life with a basket on his back,\r\nand now, though practically indistinguishable from a born gentleman,\r\nshowed his origin to keen eyes in an impeccable neatness of dress, lack\r\nof freedom in manner, extreme cleanliness of person, and a certain\r\nindescribable timidity and precision with his knife and fork which\r\nmight be the relic of days when meat was rare, and the way of handling\r\nit by no means gingerly.\r\n\r\nThe two parties who were strolling about and losing their unity now\r\ncame together, and joined each other in a long stare over the yellow\r\nand green patches of the heated landscape below. The hot air danced\r\nacross it, making it impossible to see the roofs of a village on the\r\nplain distinctly. Even on the top of the mountain where a breeze played\r\nlightly, it was very hot, and the heat, the food, the immense space,\r\nand perhaps some less well-defined cause produced a comfortable\r\ndrowsiness and a sense of happy relaxation in them. They did not say\r\nmuch, but felt no constraint in being silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we go and see what\xe2\x80\x99s to be seen over there?\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur to\r\nSusan, and the pair walked off together, their departure certainly\r\nsending some thrill of emotion through the rest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn odd lot, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought we should never get\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em all to the top. But I\xe2\x80\x99m glad we came, by Jove! I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nmissed this for something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t _like_ Mr. Hirst,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan inconsequently. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvery clever, but why should clever people be so\xe2\x80\x94I expect he\xe2\x80\x99s awfully\r\nnice, really,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, instinctively qualifying what might have\r\nseemed an unkind remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHirst? Oh, he\xe2\x80\x99s one of these learned chaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur\r\nindifferently. \xe2\x80\x9cHe don\xe2\x80\x99t look as if he enjoyed it. You should hear him\r\ntalking to Elliot. It\xe2\x80\x99s as much as I can do to follow \xe2\x80\x99em at all. . . .\r\nI was never good at my books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith these sentences and the pauses that came between them they reached\r\na little hillock, on the top of which grew several slim trees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you mind if we sit down here?\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, looking about him. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njolly in the shade\xe2\x80\x94and the view\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d They sat down, and looked straight\r\nahead of them in silence for some time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do envy those clever chaps sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsuppose they ever . . .\xe2\x80\x9d He did not finish his sentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see why you should envy them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, with great\r\nsincerity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd things happen to one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cOne goes along smoothly\r\nenough, one thing following another, and it\xe2\x80\x99s all very jolly and plain\r\nsailing, and you think you know all about it, and suddenly one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow where one is a bit, and everything seems different from what it\r\nused to seem. Now to-day, coming up that path, riding behind you, I\r\nseemed to see everything as if\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he paused and plucked a piece of grass\r\nup by the roots. He scattered the little lumps of earth which were\r\nsticking to the roots\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAs if it had a kind of meaning. It\xe2\x80\x99s because I love\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven while they had been saying commonplace things Susan had been\r\nconscious of the excitement of intimacy, which seemed not only to lay\r\nbare something in her, but in the trees and the sky, and the progress\r\nof his speech which seemed inevitable was positively painful to her,\r\nfor no human being had ever come so close to her before.\r\n\r\nShe was struck motionless as his speech went on, and her heart gave\r\ngreat separate leaps at the last words. She sat with her fingers curled\r\nround a stone, looking straight in front of her down the mountain over\r\nthe plain. So then, it had actually happened to her, a proposal of\r\nmarriage.\r\n\r\nArthur looked round at her; his face was oddly twisted. She was drawing\r\nher breath with such difficulty that she could hardly answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might have known.\xe2\x80\x9d He seized her in his arms; again and again and\r\nagain they clasped each other, murmuring inarticulately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Arthur, sinking back on the ground, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s the most\r\nwonderful thing that\xe2\x80\x99s ever happened to me.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked as if he were\r\ntrying to put things seen in a dream beside real things.\r\n\r\nThere was a long silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the most perfect thing in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d Susan stated, very gently\r\nand with great conviction. It was no longer merely a proposal of\r\nmarriage, but of marriage with Arthur, with whom she was in love.\r\n\r\nIn the silence that followed, holding his hand tightly in hers, she\r\nprayed to God that she might make him a good wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what will Mr. Perrott say?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked at the end of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear old fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur who, now that the first shock was over,\r\nwas relaxing into an enormous sense of pleasure and contentment. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nmust be very nice to him, Susan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe told her how hard Perrott\xe2\x80\x99s life had been, and how absurdly devoted\r\nhe was to Arthur himself. He went on to tell her about his mother, a\r\nwidow lady, of strong character. In return Susan sketched the portraits\r\nof her own family\xe2\x80\x94Edith in particular, her youngest sister, whom she\r\nloved better than any one else, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept you, Arthur. . . . Arthur,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat was it that you first liked me for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a buckle you wore one night at sea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, after due\r\nconsideration. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember noticing\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s an absurd thing to\r\nnotice!\xe2\x80\x94that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t take peas, because I don\xe2\x80\x99t either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFrom this they went on to compare their more serious tastes, or rather\r\nSusan ascertained what Arthur cared about, and professed herself very\r\nfond of the same thing. They would live in London, perhaps have a\r\ncottage in the country near Susan\xe2\x80\x99s family, for they would find it\r\nstrange without her at first. Her mind, stunned to begin with, now flew\r\nto the various changes that her engagement would make\xe2\x80\x94how delightful it\r\nwould be to join the ranks of the married women\xe2\x80\x94no longer to hang on to\r\ngroups of girls much younger than herself\xe2\x80\x94to escape the long solitude\r\nof an old maid\xe2\x80\x99s life. Yet two figures suddenly appeared among the trees above them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s shade,\xe2\x80\x9d began Hewet, when Rachel suddenly stopped dead. They\r\nsaw a man and woman lying on the ground beneath them, rolling slightly\r\nthis way and that as the embrace tightened and slackened. The man then\r\nsat upright and the woman, who now appeared to be Susan Warrington, lay\r\nback upon the ground, with her eyes shut and an absorbed look upon her\r\nface, as though she were not altogether conscious. Nor could you tell\r\nfrom her expression whether she was happy, or had suffered something.\r\nWhen Arthur again turned to her, butting her as a lamb butts a ewe,\r\nHewet and Rachel retreated without a word. Hewet felt uncomfortably\r\nshy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rachel after a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can remember not liking it either,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet. \xe2\x80\x9cI can remember\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbut he changed his mind and continued in an ordinary tone of voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we may take it for granted that they\xe2\x80\x99re engaged. As a day upon which any emotion has been\r\nrepressed is different from other days, so this day was now different,\r\nmerely because they had seen other people at a crisis of their lives.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA great encampment of tents they might be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hewet, looking in\r\nfront of him at the mountains. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it like a water-colour too\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nknow the way water-colours dry in ridges all across the paper\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nwondering what they looked like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis eyes became dreamy, as though he were matching things, and reminded\r\nRachel in their colour of the green flesh of a snail. When it became painful to look any\r\nlonger, the great size of the view seeming to enlarge her eyes beyond\r\ntheir natural limit, she looked at the ground; it pleased her to\r\nscrutinise this inch of the soil of South America so minutely that she\r\nnoticed every grain of earth and made it into a world where she was\r\nendowed with the supreme power. I like to know people\xe2\x80\x99s Christian names.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRachel,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRachel,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cI have an aunt called Rachel, who put the life\r\nof Father Damien into verse.
